Tales of the Early Days.
No. XVI.—The One Chance of Lifer Harley.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BY PRICE WARUNG.
To every man comes his one chance. And
Convict Harley, absolutely (so it seemed) despised
by Fortune, was yet offered his. The boon of free-
dom was in his hands for one golden moment.
The thing for which he had hungered and thirsted,
for which he had laboured and sweated, for which
he had paid deference to vulgarity and been obse-
quious to vice, was in his possession. The glory
and the joy of Liberty were his —his—his !
Nevertheless, he threw it aside as something
that was worth nothing—as though it was of no
more value than the word of honour of a convict
overseer or the honesty of a lawyer.
* # #
The assigned servant of Mr. Philip Fleury,
the able and eloquent and detestably scoundrelly
Sydney auctioneer, Harley had congratulated
himself when from the post of book-keeper that
btilliant financier and unscrupulous rogue, his
iriaster, had promoted him to the managership of
his valuation branch. Fleury knew men and was
perfectly sure that Harley’s first crime had not
been succeeded by others. The sudden yielding
t) temptation which had occasioned his “being
sent out ” had also entailed so measureless a
heritage of suffering and remorse as to render him
impregnable to similar attacks thenceforward.
Fleury really thought so, and Harley, while
at first dubious of his own powers of resis-
tance, yet in his heart of hearts believed it.
The one fall, even when accentuated by the
thousand and one officially favoured immoralities
Of the Bystem, did not invariably kill the better
part Of d convict. Occasionally it strengthened
him because it tore away the veil which had hid
bis weakness from his own vision.
Harley, then, went in and out among the stained
souls which made up the major part of Sydney
population in the Thirties,—seeking to keep him-
self uneontaminated by the contact. Among
the wealthy villainy it was that Mr. Philip Fleury
found his chief patrons. Land and house and
shop property were becoming valuable in Sydney,
and enterprising gentlemen of the Fleury stamp,
having a fine instinct for smelling out a rascal who
Would “ eokuin ” a land-grant or who could induce
the Original grantees to disappear mysteriously
(rtevef to re-appear except in the form of a
skeleton in a Blue Mountain gully), had a
large and profitable clientele among the specu-
lators in “uueaniol increment.” And Harley,
being the confidential agent of a most expert
manipulator in most kinds of privileged roguery
and in many that were nut privileged, might
have made his fortune five times over had he
chosen to exert in his own interest the skill and
knowledge which he exercised on behalf of bis
master. But for himself ho would not lie; though
he would suppress material evidence in preparing
statements for the Board of Assignments and the
Executive Council. Again and again he knew he
was making false statements, and compiling wil-
fully erroneous “abstracts,” but the salve to his
conscience was that he employed no more
than the precise words of his instructions.
He was but an “ assigned servant ” without voli-
tion or voice of his own, and bound by a hundred
regulations to obey without demur or enquiry,
the command of the man into whose charge the
System, in a generous regard for his moral well-
being, had given him. Therefore, he reasoned,
the culpability was not his, but his master’s.
Let him, however, profit himself by such acts as
he was almost daily compelled to perform for his
master’s benefit, and he would be —once more—
a criminal. The circumstance that the act
might be no more heinous than several which
were weekly given effect to by (and for) the
Honorable the Executive Council did not warp
his judgment. To every man his conscience, and
if His Honor the Lieutenant-Governor, and the
Venerable the Archdeacon —there was as yet no
Bishop to press the episcopal signet to the lump
of wax which was to sanctify and legalise an act
of officialised scoundrelisra—chose to shut their
eyes to dubious proceedings while opening their
mouths to receive the golden reward of complicity,
that was their look-out. He, the continued
assigned servant, would share neither the sin nor
the profit. When, some morning, he would open
the Gazette and find his name in the list of
“ Conditional Pardons,” he would go forth to the
world a sobered and chastened and honest man.
This was his dream and his ambition. And every
fresh instruction to do this and that shady thing
he regarded as a fresh trial of his moral power.
He did these things, but the force of his inward
protest grew with each repetition. Fleury con-
gratulated himself upon his clerk’s increasing
pliancy and bility, while the clerk himself felt
that at his core was an increasing strength. And
the latter, the duller of the two, was truer in his
judgment.
# * *
“ Harley,” said Fleury one day,
a ticklish thing to put through with the Surveys
and the Council—deucedly ticklish. I want your
best attention, and no mistake.”
“Yes, sir !”
“It’s a block of Cook’s River land, a full
hundred acres —a mile or so ’tother side the
Waterloo mills, an’ runnin’ to the river. Swampy
in parts, but easily drained—just the thing for
market-gardeners an’ fodder-growers—near town
—same sort of ground as brings thirty-quid a year
rent at Isleworth an’ Brentford, in the old
country. Now, I’ve a buyer for that at thirty-
three-ten an acre—a topper of a price by a tenner
over any land sold this twel’months. Are ye
followin’ me?”
“ Yes, sir !”
“Title’s a grant—grantee, ’manoipist—d’ye
wish ye were a ’mancipist, Teddy ?—never mind,
’ll come soon enough——” He liked to fire these
little shots, did Fleury, knowing in his heart the
while that if he could help it, he’d keep Harley
on till he grew grey waiting for the pardon that
would never come. But Harley, unflinching in
demeanour, though his heart quivered, only
replied with his respectful, “Yes, sir !”
“ Eh, I know you would. Well, that ’mancipist
can’t be found. “ He went a-whalin’, an has never
turned up,”
“That should be easy enough then, sir, to get
over,” said Harley, who had the clearest of recol-
lections of certain trifling incidents in the history
of colonial land-grabbing.
“ Not so easy as you’d think, Jim. If the cove
had left no one behind in possession, the thing
would be easy, but that’s just what the fellow did.
There’s a woman in possession who says she’s his
wife.”
“ That’s bad—for you, sir,” said Harley, falling
into his master’s humour.
“ Aye, damned bad. An’ wus’, she’s got her
papers—marriage lines —her man’s pardon—the
muster-master’s permission to marry when he was
on’y a ticket cove. I know that much.”
“ How did you find out, sir, may I ask ?”
“ Oh, easy ! Sent up Izzy Chipman, the cop,
to bounce her into showin’ ’em. Izzy said he’d
information as she was a loose ’un. She got
indignant then, an’ show’d her lines. Damn her !
if she ’adn’t ’em, I’d ’ave her out in ’arf-a-jiffy.”
“ How d’you mean then, sir, to get her off the
land ? I s’pose that’s th’ object ?”
“ Yes—that's the game ! I knew you’d see the
point, Jim. An’, look ’ere, the cove’s wots in
this with me is pretty ’igh up in the service, an’
you’ve on’y to do wot I say ” He stopped to
leisurely withdraw a cheroot from his pouch and
light it. But the while he did so, he was sending
swift, searching glances angularly at Harley’s
silent face. The clerk was breathless. The tone
more than the words implied—ah, so much !
“ An’ ” Fleury puffed; each expiration of
smoke was to Harley as the thrusting fork of an
adder’s tongue to a victim caged with the noxi-
ous thing.
“ And what, sir?” Harley could not bear the
strain.
“ You get your ” He paused again, with a
subtle ingenuity worthy of the devil-born, to
emit more coils of smoke.
“ My ticket, sir?”
“No !” Another puff. “Better ’n that!”
“A pardon, sir?” The knots were tightening
on Harley’s throat.
“ Ye-es!”
“ A conditional, sir ?”
“No 1” He flung the cheroot to the corner of
the room, and beat his closed fist on the desk.
“No. Absolute /”
Absolute 1 Harley swept the perspiration away
with his handkerchief. Absolute! Free! Togo
where he chose ! Heaven within his grasp !
“ I’ll do what you want me, sir,” he, stumbling
in his utterance, said at last.
And Fleury chuckled.
# # *
“That’s right!" the auctioneer said.
“You won’t get a chance like this again in a
hurry, Harley, I can tell you. You marry the
woman ”
“ W-what—sir ?”
“ You marry the woman —you’ve agreed to do
that, ain’t ye?”
“ I didn’t know as it was that, Mr. Fleury.”
“ Well, ye said ye’d do wot I wanted, didn’t ye?
An’ I want ye to marry the ”
“ But—but, sir—her husband may be alive.”
“ oh, it, we’ll take care of that. We can’t
kill him—but we’ve got the chap ready as saw him
die.” And Fleury laughed his best polished laugh.
“ An’ s’posing the woman won’t have me ? ”
“ Now, Harley ” Fleury turned and
threateningly faced his assigned clerk. “Do ye
mean to say as ye’ve been wi’ me for a couple o’
years, an’ are still so green as to let a trifle stand
in yer way. Ye’ve to marry ’er—an’ that’s all
about it, my man, or ye don’t get no pardon
through me. An’ wot’s more, I’ll stop your
chance for as much as a ticket. Ye’ll be a.lag for
life, an’ no mistake, if ye cross me ! ”
“I don’t wish to cross you, sir, but I don’t see
how I’m to marry tli’ woman against her will.”
“ Don’t ye ? Now, I do—an’ ye’ve jest to do as
I say, an’ things ’ll come out as easy as John
Palmer slapped the Guvnor’s face.* Listen, now !”
And then Mr. Fleury, privileged agent of the
Government and many highly-placed officials, un-
folded his ingenious plan.
The woman, to eke out her living, occasionally
let rooms to Sydney residents who wished to enjoy
“ the exhilariousness of Cook’s River air ” in pre-
ference to visiting Parramatta or Campbelltown.
Harley was to engage accommodation for a month
or two or so long as was necessary to secure the
ends of his employer. By employing the delicate
arts familiar to every man of the world he was to
invite the woman’s confidence, and that obtained,
and a friendly and sympathetic feeling established
between them, the concerted “news” would be
communicated to her of her husband’s death,
“I’ll have that cokumed up all right, Jim, an’
it’ll fluster her. Then all you’ve to do is to show
a friendly—a brotherly interest, ye know, in her,
an’ accordin’ as she takes to ye enough, or don’t
take to ye at all, ye’ll have to act. If she cares
for ye, she’ll marry ye as soon as ye like. If ye
don’t, ye’ve still to earn her confidence, an* then
it’s all the same. I know women, an’ I’ll bet a
hundred dollars to Garnham Blaxoell’s bill,+ she’ll
ask ye to see her papers for the property put
through the offices. She’s a baby in business, an’
she’ll do that, or I’m a Dutchman !”
“And, s’posing she won’t marry me, and I do
get the papers, that won’t help you, will it, sir ?”
“If she marries ye, all right. An’ if she don’t
marry ye, that’s all right, too. When we—that
is I, myself—give ye th’ papers back, ye’ll jest be
her’usband accordin’ to them. Now, d’ye see ?”
And Harley had to admit he perceived the logic
of the scheme. Obtain possession of the woman’s
“marriage-lines” and th© document which had
given the husband permission to marry, and
the rest was indeed easy. The register of the
marriage would have proved an obstacle to anyone
less potential in the “ departments ” than Fleury.
But for Fleury, the expenditure of a mere baga-
telle of energy sufficed to make the book-entry
correspond with a certificate which had been
forged or mutilated to serve his ends.
Solicitors of this present day sending for
evidence of early-day marriages performed by the
colonial chaplains should soon learn that, for the
gaps in the records the land speculators of the
Twenties and Thirties were almost wholly respon-
sible.
# # #
Whichever happened, the marriage or the
refusal to marry, Fleury, Harley’s consent won,
considered the land was virtually his client’s.
By the marriage, Harley, without recourse to a
criminal act, would secure the control of the land
and, of course, he would have signed a document
transferring the interest in it before the ceremony.
Without the marriage the same end would be
effected by the substitution of Harley’s name in
the two papers—say, by the hands of the gifted
Mr. Pounce, writer and forger—followed by the
* “John Palmer struck the Governor” —Oommissary-
General Palmer, though an undersized man, had a long
reach of arm. On one occasion he struck Governor King.
That incident, and his length of arm, passed into Early-
day Sydney proverbs.
f “ Garnham Blaxcell’s bill ” —a worthless bit of paper.
Blaxoell, one of the contractors for the “ Rum Hospital,”
and formerly Colonial Secretary, absconded to India,
leaving a large number of promissory notes i>n circulation.

“I have stated my conditions,” said Harley.
A sweat as of fever oozed through his pores.
“I’ve sinned enough in my time. I’ll sin no
more!” . ~, , , ~
“For the third time, ye cantin’ Methody——
lie walked across the room, and seating himself at
his desk, took out a sheet of notepaper from the
rack, dipped his quill in the ink, and then,
with suspended pen, declared his ultimatum.
“This is your one chance for freedom ! Will
you take it? Give me the papers, an’ I’ll forgive
and forget wot’s just gone by ! Will ye take it ? ”
“ No !” said Convict Harley.
# * •
In the year ’54, Convict Harley died
insane at Port] Arthur. The attractive site,
“similar,” so ran the advertisement of a later
day, when it was thrown into the market, “ to the
admirable and fertile quality of land which, rich
and capable of producing almost anything, lets for
=£3o per acre annually merely to supply Covent
Garden, London,” was incorporated with other
blocks and formed the nucleus of a famous estate.
The grant to Henry Burne was superseded by
another in which Mr. Philip Eleury was the
agent for the person who became the grantee.
Some day, when we tell the story of the Black
and White Books, we shall describe Convict
Harley’s later career.
The Reprobate’s Reply.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Three droving men, some three weeks syne,
Sat drinking the Queensland rum ;
’Twas four a.m. when twa o’ them
Saw Jock M'Phee succumb.
Hech ! they were giddy songs he’d sung,
And the yarns which he’d spun were “ free ” !
For the liquor that nioht had loosed the tongue
O’ gudeman Jock M'Phee.
They tault the meenister what befell,
So he tuk braw Jock to task•
“Jock, gie me noo an answer true
To one question I wull ask.
“ An’ it happened the Laird had stricken yo,
A reprobate, graceless mon,
Whan ye war a bletherin’ yestere’en
D’ye ken whare ye wad hae gone ?”
“ Whare wad I hae gone?”—and Jockie wunk—
“ Dinna ye fash yersel* mair—■
For I wad ha’ bin too terrible drunk
To ha’ gone anywhere.”
THE BREAKER.
The Age now asserts that “ the system of
importing officers has, on the whole, proved a
failure.” A sentiment eloquently seconded by all
those parents whose daughters have not succeeded
in making the necessary “ impression.”
The Age discloses the fact that, for some
considerable time, the Nelson man-of-war has
been connected with Melbourne by telephone.
An agitation for gas and water is held over, pend-
ing an answer from the Department to a request
that city sites be placed at the disposal of the
fleet. The Imperial officers find it beastly incon-
venient getting home from the club.
What One Does in ’Em.
Dear Bulletin,— The story “ What he did with
it,” in your issue of the 29th October, was written
by a man who knows something. That unknown
(to me) writer is from this day my friend. In the
interest of those who may be afflicted with dipso,
mania or alcoholism, I have to state that the
ignorance, real or pretended, of judges, justices,
or others, is the cause of many an irreparable error.
Notably Judge Striker. I have heard him say :
“He must have been sober when lie forged this
cheque.” There is no must in it. Ido not defend
drunkenness, nor would I allow a murderer to
escape who, in a sober manner, slew his neighbour'
whilst he “had ’em.” But, as I told you once
before, I wrote, directed, and posted to you a
story for which you paid me and I have no recol-
lection of the facts. Still, the matter was readable
and payable, and I can swear to its emanation
from me.
All glory to the coming cheap, portable micro-
phonograph which will be used in police and civil
cases. Those who wish to destroy their identity
or swindle their employes will then have to speak
in assumed voices. In the “Moonstone,” the stealer
of the diamond has to be brought into his former
state of ebriety and surroundings in order to
cause the required somnambulism. Just in the
same way, in order to recall lost facts it would be
often advisable to make a man drunk. This and
snakebite are perhaps the only justifiable cases of
premeditated drunkenness ; in fact the pre-ordin-
ance of it ought not to be in every instance known
to the patient, victim, experirnentee, or whatever
you like to call him.
When the Drink Commission was sitting, I
urged on the Government and public the great
good that could be done by calling a few profes-
sional tipplers and beer-eaters as witnesses instead
of Bishop Barry, Dr. Mackellar, &c. I was the
more annoyed at the want of sympathy shown me,
as I hoped to be a summoned expert, especially
having just had a remarkable experience verifying
my hypotheses.
It was thusl had only nine pounds after
shearing, but was determined to see the Smoke.
I therefore sent that sum to Seymour P. 0., and
started to swag it towards my money. Luck
befriended me, and I worked a passage from
Balranald to Echuca in the Native Companion.
A few days carried me to my bank, and I arrived
in Melbourne safe, with about 30 shillings in my
pocket and seven pounds in my swag. I left the
latter at a boarding-house, and proceeded to invest
my loose cash in samples. This was at night.
Next morning I found myself in Spencer-street
without money or even a hat, and I had forgotten
where the lodging-house was. All I remembered
was that it had a red lamp.
In front of me a man was washing a buggy in a
shed belonging to a coach-factory.* I asked him for
for an old hat. He answered in Scotch. Result, a
hat, and loan of two shillings. Then I remem-
bered the “Moonstone.” How I was muddled
when I arrived by the late train. Six long beers
made me in statu quo ante and I proceeded to the
railway station. Coming out from the platform,
I walked straight to a house in Bourke-street with
a red lamp and a German landlady. There was
my swag in a corner untouched, and my joy was
like unto that of the seraphs over a repentant
lushington. We want a new Drunk Commission.
I am ready.
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 10, 1892.
Blood versus Bricks.
The dismal spectre of the ancient virtue of
Thrift rose again last weekin the New South
Wales Legislative Council, and the noise of
the same old mummies beating out the same
old straws of logic is once more heard in the
land. The visible cause of the trouble is
that feeble and inconsequent legislator,
attorney Trickett, M.L.A. ; and the shape
it has taken is that of an amendment on the
Assembly’s Bill for the abolition of plural
voting, the said amendment providing that
the plural vote shall not be abolished, but
that every man of Property shall have one
suffrage for his manhood and one more for
his greed and parsimony and his capacity
for getting ahead of his fellow-men. In
other words, the principle of One-man-one-
vote is to be altered to read that every man
shall have one vote and some of them shall
have more ; and that, all men are to be free
and equal, only some of them are to be more
equal than others. And in support of this
contention the old, old story of the beauties
of Thrift is to lie told all over again, that
grasping and niggardly “virtue” being the
only one which appeals to the sordid
souls of the apoplectic nominees. No one
has ventured to propose, so far, an extra
vote for honesty, courage, generosity, truth-
fulness, patriotism, genius, industry, know-
ledge, inventive capacity, for faith, hope,
charity, or any other good or noble quality.
The Upper House cares nothing at all for
any of these, but is solid as one enlarged
liver on the necessity of encouraging Pro-
perty. If it has even the courage of live per-
cent. of its opinions the Electoral Bill is
already as good as lost.
* * *
In speaking of the proposed extra vote for
Thrift, we have used, for convenience sake,
the same cant term which the National Ass,
and the Council, which is that animal’s
servant and representative, habitually em-
ploy. In reality, however, it is something
lower and meaner than even that poor, mean
quality which the nominees propose to
reward with a double share of political
power'. Thrift is a base and grovelling, yet
comparatively honest quality. It is tire art
of going in rags and broken boots itr order
to scrape farthings together, and stow away
shillings in a stocking hidden up a chimney.
It is the complete mystery of being dirty in
order to save the price of soap, of being
ignorant, unintelligent, and animal, to save
the cost of education, of living on coarse
food, of inhabiting a hovel, of working early
and late, and of sitting in the dark to save
candle-ends. It is the noble trait of the
Dutch peasant who harnesses his wife to the
plough, and of the Chinaman who sells his
partner irr life and starts business with the
proceeds—in short, it is the epitome of petty
meanness, and the whole duty of hide-bound
selfishness condensed into one word. But it
does not necessarily imply direct robbery or
actual fraud, and therefore, in a certain
negative sense, it may almost pass for a
virtue. Unhappily, however, Thrift is
seldom the road to wealth. The thrifty
individual generally ends by putting his
savings in some villainous institution at a
high percentage, and is plundered. All the
half-hundred insolvent banks of Melbourne
and Sydney lived on Thrift, and since their
general bankruptcy that good and noble
quality has mostly gone to the Benevolent
Asylum.
« # »
Therefore, as the Council has no reverence
for anything but financial success, it does
not propose, despite all its wasted eloquence,
to give anybody a vote for Thrift. What it
really does propose is to give a vote for
affluence. It will give the usurer one
suffrage because he is a man, and one more
because he has habitually sold the bed from
under the widow, and has invested the pro-
ceeds in houses or land. It will give the
bogus banker an extra suffrage because he
successfully embezzled the deposits, while
the depositor, whose vanished hoard pro-
bably represented a whole lifetime of
“thrift,” will Tget no extra suffrage at all.
If the usurer had been too tender-hearted
to enrich himself by ejecting old women into
the stony highways, or the banker had been
too honest to steal the deposits, neither
ivould get a property vote, therefore the one
is allotted a vote for his brutality, and the
other for his dishonesty. Political power is
to be, as heretofore, the reward of wealth,
whether that wealth is acquired by nigger-
driving, land-dummying, fraudulent insol-
vency, bogus banking, arson, adulteration,
illicit - distillation, usury, cattle - stealing,
mine-salting, running an opium-den of fan-
tan house, welshing, bookmaking, receiving
stolen property, prize-fighting, running dis-
honest “ totes,” or any other form of robbery
or brutality under the sun. But there is no
question of a vote for Thrift ; it is baldly
and undisguisedly a vote for money, no
matter how acquired or how r applied—one
vote for each of the eleven disciples, and
two for Iscariot, because he had sold his
Master to advantage and become a capitalist
thereby.
# # *
There is not, The Bulletin firmly
believes, more than one man in the Council
who will give an honest and willing support
to the central principle of the Electoral Bill
—not more than one, if there is one, who
does not hunger after the blessed privilege
of a vote for Property. But the Upper
House is an uncertain and feeble body, and
much gone in the knees, and if it realises
that the Assembly is in deadly earnest, it
will pass the measure all the same. If it
has doubts about the sincerity of the Lower
House it will probably patch and tinker the
Bill, and kill its vital principle by inserting
the proviso that the fat man shall have an
extra suffrage for his pile, no matter how
acquired, and for his corpulence ; and ivill
their return it to the people’s representatives
so late in the session that it will stand a fair
chance of being lost in the general confusion.
And if it is tolerably certain that the Assem-
bly is not in earnest, it will reject the
measure in toto , and announce its resolute
adherence to the grand old rule that the rich
man shall have twenty votes if he is rich
enough, and forty if he can get them. It is
difficult to form an accurate estimate of the
situation, but the balance of probabilities is
that the Lower House is not in earnest.
Also, the Ministry is only half in earnest,
and the Ministerial organ is not in earnest at
all, and the rest of the “respectable” press of
Sydney is almost undisguisedly hostile. The
one thing which forces the Assembly to pre-
serve an aspect of artificial enthusiasm is the
fact that there is a large majority of flesh
and blood in every electorate in the province
which favours electoral reform, and the least
sign of wavering means absolute ruin in a
political sense. But the conversion of at
least two-thirds of the legislators has been
much too sudden to be altogether real.
There is not, we firmly believe, one lawyer
in the House, one tquatter, merchant,
banker, landlord, or any other form or
variety of capitalist who, in his heart, does
not loathe the very sound of what the pre-
tendedly friendly capitalistic press sneeringly
calls “ the Democratic shibboleth,” and who
does not long to see the hated measure sunk
in the lowest depths of eternal oblivion.
Out of 141 legislators there are probably 100
who will rejoice with an exceeding great joy
if some circumstance over which they don t
appear to have any control gives them
another year’s breathing space. Time and
the inert weight of fat are powerful allies,
and every postponement means more hope
for the future.
# * *
The first definite sign of possible treachery
has come from the Australian Star the
special organ of the Dibbs Cabinet —which
asks the Council, in lukewarm tones, not to
precipitate a crisis which might possibly
compel a dissolution of the Assembly and all
the worry and turmoil of a new election.
The only plausible explanation of this
deliverance is that it is considered desirable
to gradually impress upon the public that a
dissolution is the only possible alternative,
should the Electoral Bill be rejected. In
reality such an expedient, under such cir-
cumstances, would be nothing else than an
act of the meanest treachery. It is not the
Assembly, but the Council, which stands in
need of dissolution ; the idea of promoting a
measure by dissolving the House which gives
a unanimous vote in its favour, while the
House which is almost unanimously hostile
remains intact, is merely shooting one’s
allies to overawe the enemy. There is no
moral effect to be gained ; at last election the
province returned an Assembly which was
pledged as one man to the abolition of plural
voting, and it cannot by any possibility do
more : there is nothing more unanimous
than absolute unanimity. The only possible
result to be produced by a general election
is that the new organisations of Capital might
have a chance to make themselves felt; that
the magnates of the National Assmight succeed
in once more forming a Conservative party
in the Lower House ; and that there would
consequently be some colour of plausibility
in the assertion that the Democratic move-
ment was a mere transitory agitation. Then
the House of Living Death, which ventures
now to oppose itself to the votes of all the
people’s representatives, would gain new
strength, and its fatty heart would rise to
the occasion. There is nothing—absolutely
nothing—to be gained for the cause which
the Assembly and the Dibbs Government
and the Dibbs Government’s organ are under-
stood to have at heart by a dissolution, and
there is everything to be lost. The odds
in the political gamble are exactly 141 to
nothing, and there is no semblance of profit
in laying such odds as these for mere amuse-
ment.
# * *
The Ministry—if it is really sincere— made
its first great mistake when it omitted to
designate the measure as being distinctly a
Bill for the Abolition of Plural Voting, by
which device it would have made it impos-
sible to tack on any proviso in favour of a
vote for alleged Thrift. It will make its last
mistake—the last it will ever have a chance
to make—if it dissolves the House which
supports its programme in order to get level
with the House which does not. If the
Council takes its courage in both hands and
rejects the Electoral Bill, there are only two
honest and downright courses available.
The one is to tack the electoral scheme on
to the Appropriation Bill, and compel the
nominees either to accept it as it stands
or take the responsibility of stopping the
business of the country, leaving the Civil
Service unpaid, the public works paralysed,
and the public creditors unsatisfied. The
other is to swamp the Council with enough
Democrats to carry the measure, and then to
settle the vexed problem in one act by the
avoirdupois weight of the newly-created
majority. There are absolutely no other
alternatives, and if the Ministry adopts
neither of these, then there is no vestige of
sincerity in G. Republican Dibbs and his
colleagues, and their official days shall be
short in the land, and it will be time for
someone else to try his hand at the great
work of rnisgovernment in their stead.
A Slander Rebutted.
It is really unfair to tlie devoted adherents of
Royalty that the subject of t he betrothal of the
Princess May of Teck to Prince George of
Wales should be still dealt with by hints and
unofficial intimations. Loyalists are kept in a
miserable condition of uncertainty with respect
to the attitude which it may be proper for them
to assume. They are, up to the time at
which we write, entirely at a loss whether
to denounce the rumour as a base caLunny
devised by wicked, unscrupulous radicals and
republicans, always eager to represent royalty
in an odious light, or whether to acclaim the
alleged betrothal as a charming instance of
the domestic virtues of the Queen and the
devoted attachment of the Princess to—well,
to the Royal Family. Any doubt respecting a
matte; of so much importance is productive of
the most serious embarrassment. But, really,
there ought to he no place for doubt in a truly
loyal heart. The current reports which are
afloat to the effect that the Princess May will
marry Prince CleorGe are merely trials of the
faith of true believers in Monarchy. No person
who has a genuine belief in the Monarchical
system and in the impeccableness by the grace
of Cod of the Sovereign, our beloved Queen,
that exemplar of all the domestic virtues,
that model to all wives and mothers,
can possibly hesitate to i;epol a rumour so
charged with scandalous depreciation of every-
thing that is worthy of allegiance and respect.
Those who hesitate or doubt are the Thomases
among loyalists, and ought to be ashamed of
themselves. There are a few facts, admitted
by every right-thinking person to be incontro-
vertible, which, when considered, show that the
alleged arrangement is impossible, and cannot
ever have been so much as thought of by any
but those scurrilous persons who invent slanders
for the base and wicked purpose of besmirching
the pui’e and elevated characters of Her
Majesty the Queen, the Prince of Wales, and
all the Royal Family —as the Prayer-book
phrases it -and also of the Batten bergs, the
Teoks, and other serene personages enholied by
matrimonial connection with that sacred and
saintly race.
# * *
It is admitted, for example, that although
there may possibly, and in a limited sense, have
been sovereigns and their sons and daughters of
the House of Hanover whose marital relations
have unhappily afforded some opportunity for
uninformed persons with ill-regulated minds to
make injurious and disreputable reflections, and
whose general morality and principles have not
shone with that clear, brilliant, and steady
flame which gratifies the loyal eye, such
deficiencies have only been attributable-
even by the most scurrilous —to personages
after they have been dead. It is, or should
be, sufficient to point out that those ghouls who
have, as it were, torn out of the grave the
reputations of buried princes, have no real, sub-
stantial endorsement from witnesses who lived
contemporaneously with the aspersed person-
ages. It would be easy to produce an over-
whelming host of testimony of the persons
highest in rank and consideration, to show that
while living these slandered persons were held
in veneration and esteem. There has not been
one of these posthumously depreciated princes
who failed to attract the reverential deference,
and to enjoy, when so disposed, the personal
intimacy of the most estimable contemporaries.
It should suffice to silence the malice ot censors,
simply to remember that in no single instance
did an archbishop or a bishop ever refuse to
associate with one of them, much less feel called
upon to reprimand them for failure to observe
those decencies and moralities so essential
towards setting an example to the nation. It
may be further adduced that no respectable
journalist ever assailed them, and that, in fact,
whenever a writer did attempt to slander their
pure and sensitive character, he was almost
certain to be a mere gaol-bird—immediately
afterward. Moreover, a close investigation of
the records of the time will show that these
very princes, however sadly maligned by a
subsequent generation, were esteemed for their
splendid qualities in every part of the country.
In their occasional progresses through the
realm, the mayors of cities, and all that was
respectable by position in the most widely
separated localities, concurred in hastening to
testify, by public addresses, to the universal
appreciation of the remarkable virtues of these
personages.
* * «
But even if there were a semblance, however
fallacious, of truth in the disgraceful aspersions
which have been thrown on the ancestors of
the present Royal family, it is certain that
nothing of the kind attaches to those of the
present day. It would be nothing short of
indecency to question their possession of all the
virtues which embellish humanity and con-
stitute the highest elevation of intellect, feel-
ing, and morality. If there be one claim
which, more than another, her present gracious
Majesty, Queen Victoria, has upon the devo-
tion of her subjects, it lies in the pure and
noble example which she has set and furnished
in all matters connected with the sanctity of
the marriage state and the maintenance of the
homely domestic feelings. It would, therefore,
be most disloyal and scandalous to attach credit
for one moment to so disparaging a supposition
as that she would countenance so indecent a
transaction as a pretended transference of
affection by a love-lorn young woman, from one
brother to another, If it were credible that a
being so tender, so refined, so devoted as the
Princess May, were capable of conceiving within
a year a marital affection for Prince (leorge
—the terrible possibility obtrudes itself that
even had her first love lived and wedded her,
the same change might have affected the move-
ments of her heart, and the wife of the Duke of
Clarence would have become enamoured of her
husband’s brother, the Duke of York. It is
wrong, it is almost criminal, to entertain the
thought that the current report can have any
origin save in the perverted imagination of the
libeller-s of royalty. It is but a short year since
the loyal hearts of the nation vibrated in pain-
ful response to the officially guaranteed sobs < f
the heart-broken princess, as clinging to the
body of the royal youth who had won her
virgin affection, she cried (as per cablegram),
“ Oh, Eddie, Eddie, come back to me !” It is
cruelly grotesque to imagine that if the Prince
were now to respond to that piteous pleading
he would find the maiden pouring out the
wealth of her young affections upon his brother.
# * *
It has been, we are aware, common enough in
other Royal and Imperial families, for marriages
to be conducted merely as incidents connected
with diplomatic exigencies, and for a princess
to be flung into a negotiation with the same
disregard of her feelings, of the inclinations of
her destined spouse, and of the alleged sanctity
of marriage, as though she were a province, or
a city. It was thus, for example, that the Arch-
duchess Maria-Louisa of Austria was flung to
Napoleon Bonaparte, as among some bar-
barous races, maidens are thrown to savage
beasts, to appease or divert their ferocity.
But every Briton knows that nothing of the kind


lias been permitted by the present occupant of
the Throne. They know that the late gifted
Duke of Clarence returned the life-long love of
the Princess May with a touching and even poetic
devotion. They know, too, that when the Prince
of Wales placed his youthful daughter in the
arms of his venerable comrade, the Duke of
Fife, it was merely in compliance with the
earnest wishes of her young heart, which
yearned for a spouse over whose declining
hairs she might watch with a wife’s, and almost
daughter’s, affection. We know, from Her
Majesty’s own published journal, that the
Princess Louise felt the first awakenings of
love when Lobne was presented to her, and
that silly stories about constraint and secret
natural girlish inclination for the young clergy-
man must be a lie—because., if it weren’t, the
Queen’s own narrative would be a fraudulent
imposition upon the credulity of her readers.
# *
These facts being known, there can be no
room for doubt about the falsity of the reported
transference of the intense and disinterested
affection of the Princess May from the late to
the present Heir-presumptive to the Crown.
Were it otherwise, what a lurid reflex light
would be cast back upon all precedent matri-
monial affairs in the family ! English parents
■do not sell their daughters for a fortune —at
ileast hardly ever. And the reason why business
-is thus banished from affairs of the heart and
True Love conquers, is because of the beautiful
(example set by our own noble Queen with re-
spect to her own family. (She may not be super-
'latively clever, she may not have the masculine
iforce of an Elizabeth, but she reigns in the
(hearts of her people by virtue of her constant
and consistent practice and maintenance of
ithose homely domestic virtues so seldom res-
pected by the occupant of a throne.
PLAIN ENGLISH.

A Suspicious-Looking Article.
.[ Brassey, the widowed cruiser who recently
(married again, lias published an article in
favour of an Imperial Council of Defence,
whereat representatives of the kawlinies, aftpr
being carefully knighted, iriay assist represen-
tatives of Great Britain to devise means for
maintaining the Empire intact. Brassey has
just sufficient brains, it seems, to despair of
establishing a zollverein between the dear old
mother country and her Protectionist “depen-
dencies.” But as regards defence. —well, the
interests of all parts of the h’Empire are abso-
lutely identical, therefore a bond of union can be
fixed up right away, prattles Brassey. A lucid
statement concerning the mechanism of this
desirable Council of Defence —a simple idea of
the end to be accomplished and the benefits it
will create—is not expected from fervid
“ loyalists ”of the Brassey type. He may be
■dreaming of nothing more than a nauseous
scheme for strengthening military spirit (which
is considerably underproof just now) in “Her
Majesty’s outlying possessions.” Or he may
have the Tory impudence to conceive the possi-
bility of an Imperial Defence Fund into which
the kawlinies can pay money for the Queen’s
fat cousin, George of Gampridge, to invest in
experimental uniforms and new buttons for the
standing army. But no matter what Brassey
really means by his article, he has said
enough to confirm the opinion that lie is
not worth listening to. Nothing is more
certain than the fact that some portions
of the Empire in question care not a straw
about the “ safety ” (as Jingoes call it) of
other portions. Canada may annex herself to
America next week, and Australia will wish
that cold-and-hot country good luck in the
transaction. British South Africa, or any
other slice of the Bark Continent, can haul
down the Union Jack and become double Dutch
for all that we are interested in the matter.
Fifty islands, scattered all over the globe,
would worry us not a bit if they ceased to sing
“God Save the Queen” of their own accord,
or were ordered to change their tune by a ruth-
less “foreigner.” Newfoundland might go
over to the French without making us turn a
hair, and the destiny of St. Lucia or Mauritius
concerns us not. The niggers would continue
to make sugar with their feet under any cir-
cumstances, and we could go on buying it from
somebody at the same old price, whatever
happened. The preservation of India to
England is a highly important financial
matter for the last - named party ; but
its indirect advantages to Australia
are not worth mentioning. Finally we
come to the Maternal Relative herself —
Britannia, the Pride of the Ocean, &c.—and
even her state of defence causes us little
anxiety. For the past 800 years nobody has
landed at Hastings to seize the country, and if
anyone were to march on London just now the
“submerged tenth” would “go” for the
enemy’s commissariat department until the in-
vasion resembled friend Jonah who was eaten up
by the whale. The indifference of Australia to
other parts of the h’Empire is generally re-
ciprocated. Great Britain will support the
southern continent as a commercial specula-
tion, just as long as it is a paying speculation —
but no longer. If necessary Hingland will fight
to preserve a profitable market for her goods
and cash. The influential wind-bags of this
rogue-ridden region are persuaded that without
Hingland we should be lost—inasmuch as they,
the town vultures feasting on a rotten condition
of things brought about by too much borrowed
money, would be lost. But already Australia is
nearly as independent of the Crown which adopted
our country for a convict settlement as America
was when (George Washington made her free.
Long before the “Home” Government has
ceased to love this portion of the Empire from
mercenary motives there will be no further fear
on the score of Our Defences. Nay, more,
there is already no chance of Australia being
captured by a few men-of-war. We need no
representation at any council of defence or
attack. With the despatch of the Soudan
Contingent our mania for helping our mother-
in-law began and ended. Any proposal to drag
Australia into the mire of Imperial policy, and
charge her rent for a small annual participation
in Hingland’s Glory, is to be strongly depre-
cated. The h’Empire as a whole is a purely
British affair, and sufficent for each bit is the
evil thereof.

A Girl for Sale.
The baby-for-sale industry is under a cloud
just now in Sydney, but the girl-for-sale busi-
ness is starting to boom. Somebody who
apparently wants to dispose of a young female
in a bankrupt estate, or to trade her off to
close accounts, advertises in Sydney E. News :
"lA/'ANTED, Lady to Adopt useful Girl, 10; £3 re-
, V quired. 8., St., .
In other words, the girl in question is openly
advertised for sale, at the low price of 60s. She
is useful,” which evidently means that the
purchaser can get the value of his or her money
put of her, either by using her as k hard-driven
domestic servant without Avages, or by some
' other process. The ad‘. is' addressed to ladies,
either because the advertiser will only do busi-
ness with'a lady, or else because, though she is
willing to ‘ trade off the victim to anybody, a
notice “to Chinamen and others,” would be
filtogpther too glaring. What kind of lady is
likely ,tp invest in a girl bf 10 under such cir-
cumstances is a subject that is too unplea-
sant to go into details about. Probably the
lady who bought a girl for 60s. might not bo
averse to selling her again at a profit, and what
kind of person she might sell her to is a
matter for particularly disgusting conjecture.
In any case the Dibbs Government would do
well to look into that advertisement and' others
like it, and consider if it can’t do anything to the
person who sells girls, and the person who buys
them, and the Evening Babymarket which acts
as agent and go-between in this variety of trade
in human flesh. Also, it might find out whether
the girl-selling business is an old-established
industry, and whether they are sold in times of
drought by mothers who drink their children,
or whether they are bred expressly for sale
purposes. When the trade begins to slop over
into the evening papers it seems to suggest that
it is a tolerably extensive industry, and has
been conducted so long with impunity that the
girl-dealer of the period is growing reckless as
to consequences. !
Jay Gould.
Jay Gould,, cojnmonly reputed to be the second
or third richest man on earth, died the other
day. He was worth many 'millions—ho w many
is * still mere conjecture—and as a shining
example of the grand virtue of Thrift he stands
almost unrivalled. There is a legend that,
many years ago, Gould worked honestly for
his livelihood at agriculture or some equally
humble and unprofitable employment; and this
was the only period in his life when he was of
the slightest value to humanity. For the rest
of his career he was a malignant and much-
respected cancer eating away the vitals of the
community. He made no money out of toil,
and speedily abandoned it. Then he became an
oily peddler in patent “ notions,” and gathered
together a few dollars by persuading house-
wives to speculate in curious mouse-traps and
other lumber. His first genuine “ rise,” how-
ever, was made by bold and naked forgery.
Without the aid of capital, for they had little,
and without Thrift, for they had none, but
merely by lying and superhuman gall, he and
his partner, tin 1 painted, raddled and bedia-
monded Fis;;—.m individual who looked like
the original seed Abrahams of the earth
secured control of the directorate of the Erie
Railroad, a poor, bankrupt, shoddy-built con-
cern which was barely worth owning. But in
the course of a stock-exchange gamble, the
firm got a chance to sell to Vanderbilt several
million dollars’ worth of bogus shares, and then
fled from justice with the proceeds. Later on
they succeeded in compounding this big felony,
and, returning to their old haunts, they started
business on a huge scale with the proceeds of
the robbery. A little later on, Fisk, who had
some semblance of human sympathy about him
(he kept a harem), was shot in the abdomen by
one Stokes, by reason of a difference of opinion
concerning a female, and, after that, Gould
went his way alone. His business consisted in
freezing out small shareholders, in watering
stock, and issuing false balance-sheets, and
selling valueless shares, and making up fraudu-
lent accounts —also in company-wrecking and
finance generally. He did no honest work in
all his soul-blasting career of godless selfishness,
fraud, forgery and inhumanity. He made not
one blade of grass to grow, and created
not one green spot in all the earth to
make mankind the better for his existence.
He produced nothing; he did nothing that
would not have been a thousand times better
left undone ; he created no wealth, but merely
appropriated the wealth of others ; he had not
even the much-advertised virtue of Thrift, for
while he was thrifty he remained poor, and it
was only when he started on a frantic career of
gorgeousness that he grew rich. He made, pro-
bably, more beggars and more suicides than any
other private individual of his time ; his history
is written in the gaol, the lunatic asylum, the
morgue, the cemetery, the hospitals for incur-
ables, and every other place of horror where the
ruined victims of a soulless gambler could
possibly be found. And finally he died
and went to the place—wherever it may
be —that the sons and daughters of the
horse-leech go, and, so far as the world
is aware, not one human being in all the
numberless millions of the world’s inhabitants
would have him back again at any price.
During the thirty years or so of his greatness
he was a living proof of the beneficent in-
fluences of Capitalism, and of such as he the
gods of the National Ass are constructed.

More Land-Grant Railways.
A week or two ago the Victorian Legislature
began to show the first symptomsof the land-grant
railway madness, and as the bank mania and
the land mania and the gold mania are all in a
cold and frozen state at present, and as Vic-
toria must have a mania of some kind on hand,
the time is evidently favourable for a boom of
this description. Campbell, M. L.A., brought
the matter before the Assembly, and, in the
hideous language of Speaker Bent, he put it
nicely, but the substance of his speech, when
it isn’t put nicely, is very much as follows :
(1) That Victoria can borrow no more British
money at present, and is too slavish in her
reverence for J. Bull to attempt borrowing in
any other county, and has become so utterly
saturated with the idea that no public works
can be built without Bull’s assistance,
that the idea of constructing them out of
revenue, or paying for them by issuing Govern-
ment bonds to the contractor, or by the issue
of notes of a State Bank, or by floating a local
loan, hasn’t even dawned on anybody; (2)
That more railways must be constructed, not
that the people really want them, for there
isn’t nearly enough population to supply a pay-
able traffic on the lines already existing, but
partly because a few “big” men are holding
most of the country for a rise, so that unless
the mallee lands and the Gippsland mountains
are opened up, there is no place for the small
farmer to go to ; and partly because it is neces-
sary ito keep up the expiring tail-end of the
land-boom at any cost; and partly because the
Davtes-Best-Munro type of financiers will
shortly have lto> take refuge in the Benevolent
Asylum unless something is invented whereby
a representative citizen can make money with-
out working. Therefore, Mr. Campbell sub-
mitted that it was advisable to start the land-
grant system in Gippsland ,a,nd about the Mallee
regions, and the House, thpugh it didn’t abso-
lutely commit itself on the spot,; looked favour-
ably on the idea. The odds arc i; fhat the land-
grant swindle will start sooner or later. The Vic-
torians, under the horrid incubusnf Davies and
Munro and the suave and gentle BENT.and (dicir
kind, have evidently lost the art of industry,
and when one boom bursts their only desire is
to fix up another at any cost., ~'fhe idea
settling down, as the Maorilanders did when
the Vogel bubble blew up, to of solid
toil, with small profits, and falling repts,. and
no fresh loans, and no booms, and a skin-flint
system of bitter retrenchment, is not in their
line. They are always expecting,to find seme
easy, downhill road to affluence, ,and the 1
“opening-up” of the immense mineral riches*
of Gippsland and the agricultural wealth of the
mallee land is the latest, .nostrum- Nobody
makes the slightest allusion, fo the fact that,
when these riches are opened up,” the land-'
grant syndicate will have .them—that whereas
the people now prospectively ,own them, when
the railway is made they won’t own them at all—
and that when a nation wants access to its own
riches, and has to give away the riches to get
the access, and then has to pay for the access in
addition, the situation is just about as profit-
able as that of the man who makes a living by
committing suicide. The Victorian people
have the land, and want railways to get to it,
therefore it is proposed to give away the land
in order that Someone else will have the rail-
ways. This is "all ihsanity and vexation of
spirit. If the country is resolved on keeping
its public works system going there are various
ways of doing it. It may fix up a cast-iron
betterment law, so as to reduce the cost of con -
struction to a minimum, and then to supply
that cost it might pile on a ponderous land-
tax and set aside the proceeds for rail-
way works only, or it might issue bonds to
the contractors in payment, or settle the bill in
Government bank-notes and redeem them at a
certain fixed rate annually, or float a local loan,
or else borrow the money in America or France
or anywhere, at any rate of interest up to 8 per
cent, if it can’t be got for less. And even this
last insane device would be cheaper in the end
than the great land-grant fraud. There never
was a land-grant railway yet which didn’t cost
the country 20 per cent, per annum at the lowest
estimate.
A Last Word About Clara Parker.
Like many other notorious scandals that have
disgusted Victoria since Shiels became king of
the Crown Law department, the Joseph
CLARKE-citm-CLARA Parker case is being care-
fully hushed up. Once more Shiels has shut
his weak eyes to the truth, and the outraged
Law is ignoring somebody’s guilt with steady
perseverance. An Attorney-General with an
inch of backbone and a decent sense of duty
would come as a revelation to the land of
privileged misdemeanour just, now—but no
matter. God helps only the people who help
themselves, and if the Victorian people are
satisfied to lie prone in the dust of contempt,
with the fat man sitting heavily upon their
body politic, it is a waste of time to cry “ God
help them ! ” The case of millionaire Joseph
Clarke and Mrs. Clara Parker is a remark-
able instance of Money’s license to play the fool
with the machinery of the Law— i.e. , to set
it moving without justification and stop
it suddenly in the same casual way.
Joseph Clarke swore an information of
forgery against his lady friend, whereupon the
Crown locked her up. Mysterious men came
in a mysterious manner and spirited Mrs.
Parker away from the care of tire Crown, and
when Joseph Clarke was called upon to make
good his allegation of forgery, tire fair alleged
criminal was nowhere to Jbe found. In due
course she returned to Melbourne, bringing
with her an extraordinary story of how she had
been dragged from the arms of justice at vast
expense, and, having joyously surrendered her
liberty to Jdxe almighty Crown, insisted upon
being tried for forgery. She was therefore
brought to trial —and acquitted. The jury
found in effect that the rickety millionaire
had—possibly without knowing what he was
doing—sworn incorrectly as to the origin of a
certain signature upon a certain bill for £SOOO.
The Crown was now left with a choice between
tw r o suppositions. Either Joseph Clarke was
a malignant liar trying to crush an inconvenient
woman at the country’s expense, or a befuddled
creature wdio swore at random and treated the
law with hiccupy contempt. The latter and
more charitable supposition was supported by all
the common-sense argument that could obtain a
foothold upon this slippery case. Somebody’s
money had been poured out like water to pro-
cure the disappearance of Mrs. Parker from
the scene of legal action, and of the few people
interested in the woman’s fate, pray how
many were interested in supplying, and
able to supply, the needful ? The question
as to what the Crown would elect to do under
the circumstances was soon settled. It did
nothing at all. Mrs. Parker had to be—-there
was no avoiding this piece of humbug—formally
sent back to prison on a charge of absconding
from her bail, and after a miserable pretence of
considering the matter, the Crown decided not
to proceed with this charge. It wasn’t that
the Crown had any more consideration for Mrs.
Parker now than it had when she was first
bundled into prison at the bidding of her dodder-
ing old pal. The prosecution of Mrs. Parker on
the “absconding” ticket would simply mean a
legal exposure of the circumstances that led
to her disappearance, and for the sake of
vested interests, the Crown went “cronk,”
as usual. Ere public recollection of this cause
celehre fades away, The Bulletin will endea-
vour to show how advisable it was to refrain
from taking serious action to discover the truth,
and what a monstrous thing the prosecution of
Mrs. Parker really was. On the strength of
Joseph Clarke’s statement that the writing of
his name on a certain bill wasn’t like his signa-
ture, Mrs. Parker was brought very near to a
conviction for forgery. Now, it is a matter of
notoriety in Melbourne that Joseph Clarke’s
signature is so frequently unlike his signature
that the pay-clerks at the Colonial Bank have
more than once refused to cash his cheques to
bearer over the counter, even when a certain
relative had initialled them. The signature of
Joe and the initials of that relative were, in
each case, so utterly unlike the writing of their
less shaky moments, that the bank couldn’t
recognise the hands. Formerly there was a
mutual arrangement between Clarke and his
relative to initial one another’s cheques, in
order that the Colonial Bank might have
a double chance to swear by them, but the
system broke down badly as a rule. Neither of
the pair could imitate his “own signature”
or initials. Men presenting one of Joe Clarke’s
cheques, and being refused cash payments, are
in the habit of passing it through a bank
account (their own or anybody else’s), and on
this supposed guarantee of genuineness the
cheque seems to be met in the ordinary way.
Joseph Clarke’s signature, then, is a wild,
uncertain thing, which the most solemn
Rechabite cannot always identify, and when he
stated that the name on Mrs. Parker’s bill
didn’t seem to have been written by himself, he
was practically testifying to its genuineness.
Yet the miserable, trembling fingers of this
erratic sheep-owner can be put up to his red
nose in contempt of the Victorian Crowm Law
department.
A humorist isn’t always a good humorist.
The Syrians of Redfern, Sydney, have
imported a priest from Jerusalem and an inter-
preter from Beyrout. The former’s English is
confined to the familiar inquiry, “ How are ye?”
so that he will much need the interpreter. The
latter is an accomplished English scholar eduqated
at the Syrian Protestant College, Beyrout,
where two-thirds of the professors are American
and the rest Syrians.

“Sydney case ” to which you allude in answering
“Crazy Quito’s” criticism of my story, “The

your correspondent for years, and that it was the
very necessity of verifying this particular one of
the points disclosed in the official papers relating
to the “ Grants ” which prevented the inclusion of
their story in the first series of “Tales of the
System.” published by you in 1800.— Price
Wahung.

840 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Dec. 7,1892,
My Dear Moorabinda,—
Somebody committed a robust piece of irony, done in
victuals, on the departure of an inhospitable Australian
Governor and his wife for the land whence they
came. He sent a hamper to the train for the vice-regal
refreshment en route, but instead of pdte de foie gras and
fizz the basket yielded up a few mustardless corned-beef
sandwiches, some cucumber (!) sandwiches, and six bottles
of lemonade ! And as their Excellencies closed their
eyes and munched they must have thought themselves
back again at their own buffet.
The current story re the jilted damsel of Newcastle
(N.S.W.) and the fickle sea-dog who promised to marry
her and didn’t, has all the elements of a romance, in-
cluding the stupidity. Briefly, there was a breach-of-
promise case, damages set at £3OO, and the strategic
captain, after vainly offering £2OO, settled the matter by
promising to marry the girl. But when the bridal morn
arrived there was a blank sheet of water where the
captain’s bark had rocked, and after agonised enquiriesit
transpired that the Deceiver had loaded up by double
shifts and sailed away to sea “ determined never to set
foot in Newcastle again !”
Both these people acted most absurdly. She ought to
have insisted on her damages and not let him go until
she got ’em. He ought to have married her (as she in-
sisted on it), paid her £5O or so for maintenance, and
gone away with a clear conscience to his other
wives in other ports. Instead of which she has got
neither man nor money, and he has one harbour to avoid
like the plague. There are always two morals to a breach
of promise case. The feminine one is—“ Make him pay.”
The masculine moral is Marry her.” And more espe-
cially so if you happen to be a sailor.
A German Pair started in Sydney Exhibition Building
last week, and save that the crowded stalls were Teutoni-
cally named, there were no particularly obtrusive German
points about the affair—no sauer-kraut or Frankfurt
sausages, or Fausts or Marguerites, or delikatassen hand-
lungs. The presiding buttonholers were gotten up in
clever fancy rig, but the stalls were mostly laden with the
same ogling doll and bouncing pin-cushion, for which
there seems to be a perpetual public want. A flower-stall
and the inevitable tea-and-bun buffet looked very effective
and pretty Mrs. Sahl’s art-stall made the superfluous
seem very necessary. But the most saleable thing I saw
was a "cure for wrinkles.” Half the people there ought
to have been absolutely mobbing it.
Mayoress Manning (still very nervous in public) opened
the Fair in English instead of the traditional Germain
but, after all, in so big a building all languages are pretty
well alike. There was the usual two-and sixpenny
attendance on the first day, and Admiral and the Misses
Bowden-Smith turned up to be stared at. The eldest
Miss Smith (you must never confound her with me,
remember !) wore an artistic, straight-up-and-down frock
of cinnamon-colour, trimmed with bands of brown satin
ribbon ; wide brown hat topped off with untanned
ostrich-feathers—the large committee bouquet of pale-
pink roses being seemingly built for the toilet. Our new
Admiral seems a homely and cheerful son of Neptune ; he
wears a short grizzled beard and moustache, and walks
with a pronounced quarter-deck roll. I like a potentate
who is not too dignified to stand and watch the v illainies
of Punch and Judy. A Sydney society belle cut rather a
mixed-up figure in a blue serge skirt, a black cloth
weather-jacket (it was a rainy day), and an airy black
gauze hat topped off with three big roses! To mingle
•serge and artificial flowers seems peculiarly Australian
taste.
Apropos, “ Madame de la Cour ” is now, I am told by a
London friend, head bodice-fitter (at £6OO a year) at
Poultz’s, London’s most trottie coutouriire. Madame
was once employed by a very swell Melbourne firm, but
Toorak didn’t appreciate her mouldings, and so she
left. Of course, between then and now there may have
been time to learn many things in the art of talking with
a mouth-full of pins, which, I think, is at least one of the
cleverest things dressmakers do.
After a very long run, the plain skirt and all its
mysterious modifications have to go upon the shelf and
rest for the seven years that are supposed to elapse before
an old fashion becomes new again. The latest gown is
nine yards wide round the feet, and trimmed with bands
of fur or braid several inches apart. Fur is very modish
just now even on ball-dresses, which are about the best
things to trim with it when you come to look at it. A
thick fur edging round a low-cut corsage ought to b
as good as a chest-protector to a fragile belle.
And so Princess May of Teck is to marry Prince George
of Wales—the jolly boy who had far more rope in Aus-
tralia than the closely-guarded and solemn “ Eddie.”
George, of course, is now King in prospective without
having suffered any of the espionage that made the late
Duke’s brief career as free and unfettered as that of a
Cup favourite the week before the race. Most women
probably consider her a lucky girl to bag two princes, but
(altogether apart from the question of common decency,
which would prevent any girl from marrying her dead
lover’s brother almost within a year) there doesn’t seem
much reason for anybody to jump for joy at the prospect
of marrying into the House of Hanover. The untram-
melled Duke of York is far too jolly to make a good
husband, and the future George the Fifth gives promise
of being the sort of potentate whose wife is always making
back to her ma. Someone remarked to me, the other
day, that the sweet Princess May is after the crown—no
matter whose head is in it.
What ought to be an interesting show takes place at
the Athenasum Hall, Waverley, on 14th inst., when a trial
of theatrical yearlings is to take place. Titheradge, (jun.)
Anson, (jun.) and young Emery, are to take part in a
performance of “Caste,” with Titheradge as Captain
Hawtrey, and young Anson in his dad’s pet part of
Ecoles, and Emery as D’Alroy. Amateur ladies assist the
young stagers, and it will be interesting to see how big
their daddies’ boots are for them.
The Xmas Fete (in aid of Sydney City Mission) opened
in premises on “The Block” the other Wednesday by
Mayoress Manning, went rather close to being a failure—
perhaps the deep anathemas of neighbouring shopkeepers
who objected to a fair in their very midst had something
to do with it? The most uncommon stall was run by Mrs.
L. Haigh and Miss Elsie Josephson (who, with Dr.
Frances Dick, is going to “ do ” Fiji and the New
Hebrides this summer). It was artistically crowded with
clubs, fans, mats, and all sorts of savage ornaments that
turn drawing rooms into wigwams. The most ordinary
stall sold cabbages. On the first day a society crowd
turned up, but the fact that almost everybody wore a
crinkled crepon made them look as if they were all
related to each other.
N.S.W. Women’s Branch of the Society for the Preven-
tion of Cruelty to Animals held its annual meeting the
other day. Many beautiful sentiments w'ere enunciated
from the platform, and an idiot of a reporter “ noticed
that no dead-bird trimmings disfigured any of the
bonnets : ” the poor man didn’t know that the absence of
bird-life was probably due more to the season than to the
society—the deceased tomtit only perches on winter
millinery. It was amusing to hear how everybody patted
Woman on the back—“ her influence would do so much
good in this noble work,” and so on. As a matter of
remorseless fact woman is, on the average, a lot crueller
than man—especially to horses. The average Amazon
rides or drives at top speed, no matter how hard or how
hilly the road is—and the girl who is taken out for a drive
and hasn’t to hold her baton half the time hasn’t enjoyed
it one bit. And ask any livery-stable man if he willingly
gives a “lady” a whip—also, enquire of any woman if
she hasn’t to ask for one every time she goes out! She
gets her whip, and then it’s flick, flick, flick, all the time-
just as it is when she’s driving a man.
And take her in conjunction with other living things—
leaving man, whom, perhaps, she tortures most of all—
completely out of the pic.ure. All fur-covered animals
are murdered principally to keep her warm. Beetles’-
wing embroideries are very fashionable. It was the go
not so long back to entangle fire flies'among a ball-dress,
where they died, of course, but not before they had
gleamed half the night. In my mind’s ear I can hear the
shriek of the ostrich as somebody pulls his tail feathers
out to make fa is or hat-trimmings for his natural enemy
—woman. Astraohan is made curly only by the most
horrible torture to the living sheep—one could multiply
instances indefinitely. I don’t mean to assert, of course,
that there are no humane women, but that to be a woman
doesn’t necessarily mean that you have no scalded dogs
or foundered horses on your conscience. Ask the cat in
any household what she thinks about it!
When the crisp springtime fades to summer's glow the
woman who is faddy about the change of the season, and
yet will put on a revelation bodice to go to the play in,
ought to be very careful that her chest-protector gets
tucked down low enough. At the “English Rose ” the
other night an elegant lady in a silken gown, out very
saucy about the collar-bone, had an inch of flannel “ pro-
teotor” working further up her back every time she
heaved a sympathetic sigh for the heroine’s troubles !
Which somehow reminds me of a story I was told about
a woman who went into a country store and asked for a
porous-plaster the other day. The young man showed
her several samples, which his customer examined
dubiously, and at length unburdened her soul in the
remark: “No, none o’ these will do fer me, young man.
I don’t want none o' yer moth-eaten rubbish.” And then
she departed to find a porous-plaster with no holes in it.
The engagement is announced of Miss Annie M. Sum-
merbelle, the promising young Sydney musician and
author of several bright musical compositions, to Mr.
Bertie Glasson, of Stanfield Station, Oarcoar.
The heartless tricks played on the newdy-married are
quite cruel enough to discourage nervous people from
committing matrimony at all. At a wedding, fiends tie
orange-blossoms on to the cab-horse that bears them
away, with the sole idea of making the poor things look
stupid in the eyes of the world. And they chalk “On
Honeymoon” in big letters all over their luggage, and
generally carry on in an unnatural spirit towards a couple
of people whom they have hitherto seemed to respect and
admire.
But a striking instance of presence of mind, under
embarrassing circumstances, akin to the above, was dis-
played by a recently-married widow, who particularly
objected to anybody taking her for a bride. So she
assumed a bored and chilly aspect, and “he” looked
sadly out of the train window, and nobody had the least
suspicion until he suddenly whipped out his handkerchief,
and lo ! there was a shower of rice with which some kind
friend had filled his pocket! Everybody grinned, and
started into life at once, but the ex-widdy was equal to
the occasion. “That’s the worst of attending these tire-
some weddings,” she said, languidly, “one gets as much
rice over one as the principal people! ”
At last woman is learning to call a spade a spade and
not mince matters so gingerly where matrimony is con-
cerned. Take this advt., for instance, from a Sydney
daily
T ADY (23) desires to marry for sake of a home;
widower not objected to. Veritas, G.P.O.
She puts the thing fairly and squarely as a fair business
exchange, you see. She is willing to assume the varie-
gated rose fetters of matrimony for the practical con-
sideration of three meals per diem and a water-tight roof.
She doesn’t ask for a husband with Family Herald legs
and a waxed moustache—she doesn’t specify at all what
sort of man is to go with the house. She is really willing
to marry from four rooms and a kitchen upwards, and the
proprietor can be anything in reason, evidently. But it is
strange that this eminently practical young person doesn’t
meditate going to Gaol instead. It would, if my observa-
tions are worth anything, turn out in the end to be a far
more agreeable way of getting board and lodging than the
other plan.
But it isn’t always a woman who boldly throws the
handkerchief to Hymen in the agony columns of the
newspapers. Here is an appeal, also from a Sydney
paper, which won’t be long unanswered, I’m sure :
IX/TATRlMONY.—lndustrious GENT., 26, possessing
nice station property, commodious homestead,
carriages, servants, &c., wishes to correspond with a
nice clever young Lady, Prot., in view of above.
Add. C.8.A., P. 0., Bourke.
Don’t all speak at once! One “young lady Prot.” at a
time, please !
Apropos ads., this is rather a choice example of insidious
innocence from Sydney D.T.
T ADY, well connected, would Manage gentleman’s
home, girl kept, wages no obj. Florrie, Rand-
wick P.O.
But this is obviously only another short cut to the altar.
A “lady” who advertises that she is willing to keep
house and wrestle with a servant girl, just for “ love,”
must simply be spoiling for matrimony, I should say.
Australian wit is sometimes rather personal. The other
evening a very tall, very thin, and shadowy swell was
standing like a note of interrogation awaiting the tram,
when a small newsboy struck an attitude in front of him
and said briskly: “ Ta-ta, old boy, meet you in the bone-
yard !”
The Infants’ Carnival is upon us and every public-hall
is thronged with friends and relatives and performing
cherubs and heated instructors. The State-schools are
especially prominent in the entertainment line, and
whether the idea is to have every infant looking stage-
wards before it’s got its second teeth or just to discourage
them from trying to exist without excitement is an un-
answered query.
At one public-school operatic outbreak, the other day,
a “ Gaiety ” skirt-dance by a very small girl was an item
—but unless the little victim is foredoomed to the foot-
lights her prowess in the high-kick will not be of much
use to her. Public dancing and singing and “dressing-
up” among the rank-and-file of State-school children
only unsettle them for a plain prosaic life, which will
doubtless be the fate of most of ’em. Because the world
must have household help, and what sort of a servant i 8
agirl likely to make who has been taught the ta-ra-ra kick
and operetta arias ? Certainly her kick may be a bovine
plunge and her song like the shriek of a locust, but still
she feels that the artistic world is her home—and her
soul is miles above boiling a potato.
The question of the hour is how long will domestic
help be available ? The Australian-born daughter of the
emigrated plain cook is a lady who objects to being-
called “ Mariar ” and ordered to skim the gravy, and
unless domestic service is conducted on the “ oblige me
by frying these sausages, Miss O’Flannigan” principle,
the whole nation of damsels will bolt into shops and
factories, leaving the house-wives to give and take their
orders themselves.
In America, it would appear, only immigration supplies
servants, and when that is cut off the ’Murkan matron
will either have to shake hands with her cook or go with-
out her. “The problem of housekeeping,” saith an
authority, “will never be solved in a free and democratic
country on any other basis than that of social equality.”
And the prophecy is equally applicable to Australia. For
one “servant” who advertises for a place there are ten
“ young ladies ” who would “ help in home,” and so on.
To do them justice, they always state “ wages no object,”
so they are prepared to pay for their dignity, anyhow.
But I sometimes think that if men were chiefs of the
kitchen, domestic insurrections would be fewer. Women
hate to be managed by women—and that, more than the
desire for independence (which isn’t a feminine charac-
teristic) drives them into factories and shops, where a
man is always the real head. If it were only the “ in-
equality” that was the matter, that veneered slavey, the
“lady help,” who sits at table and is introduced to every-
body, ought to be as merry as a grig.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

John Gourlay closed his season at Sydney
Garrick on Saturday, and that house is tem-
porarily deserted. John’s last night was a
delirious hash of all sorts of comedy with the in-
variable “Living Models” at the conclusion.
Mr. Cosgrove having gone away to be a fierce
Irish villain at Her Majesty’s, his former part was
taken by Mr. Scot Inglis, a pallid gentleman who
seems somewhat nervous in places, but who pro-
mises well for the future. Also, Mr. H. Shine,
having got tired of being an Irish ruffian wid a
shtick at Her Majesty’s, came back and lent a
hand. During Mr. Gourlay’s season he has intro-
duced to the people of Sydney the lady with the
shortest frock on record, also the lady whose
dress is cut lower at the neck than any other
dress ever seen in these parts, and in other ways
he has deserved well of the community. Concern-
ing his partner, Mr. Walton, The Bulletin critic
has an ancient grudge against that gentleman, and
though he has tried to view him with a kindly
eye the attempt hasn’t been a great success.
About nine years ago The Bulletin man made a
short voyage in Mr. Walton’s society, and in the
course of four days’ whist found him to be one of
the most cantankerous card-players on record.
* * *
The Bulletin has still further considered
Mr. Lionel Rignold at Her Majesty’s (Sydney),
and its opinion on that gentleman is still sus-
pended. He undoubtedly plays the part of Nicu-
demus Dickenson with very great success, but it is
not a character that admits of much originality.
There is a species of comedy that consists princi-
pally in a red-headed man hoarsely demanding a
pint of “four ’arf,” and of this is the stage-
cockney compounded. There is always a kind of
unctuous huskiness in his voice, like a man who
talks in a loud whisper through a feather-bed,
which variety of utterance is supposed to arise
from fog and gin ; he is bow-legged, red-faced,
and generally walks sideways, like a crab; he
wears strange and horrible clothes ; he is frowsy,
leery, and wildly cocksure about everything in this
world or the next, and he looks as if he slept with
the dog. And even though all London may rise
in insurrection. The Bulletin hereby records its
conviction that the best stage-cockney on record is
a poor, bald, stereotyped institution, fit only to
rank with the stage-policeman (there is not in
the whole world, there never was and
there never will oe a better stage - policeman than
Sweeney of Her Majesty’s) and the wicked
baronet, and that it is a most depressing character
to witness. The fact that Mr. L. Rignold has
played the part of Nicodemus many hundred times
in England, only makes us feel sorry for every-
body concerned. Miss Hilda Spong seems more
confident than she was on the first night, and the
scenery, which is surpassingly beautiful, grows
upon us every day; but the piece wants a new
villain as much as ever. Mr. Cosgrove could
play the nice, little curate to perfection, but he
doesn’t fit the clothes of the big, masterful villain,
and he seems to commit murder feebly somehow.
