Fillis’ circus is prospering more and more
as the days go by, and as an additional attraction
the management has turned on Mdlle. Jasia
Scherazade —apparently out of the “Arabian
Nights”—the “first lady lion-tamer that ever
appeared in the Southern Hemisphere.” Mdlle.
Scherazade is a plump and nice-looking person,
and when the common fate of her profession over-
takes her, The Bulletin will congratulate the lion
that gets the first bite. A lion that wanted to
keep himself up with a little snack could hardly
find anything more appetising than Mademoiselle
Jasia. The other Sydney shows are feeling much
as usual, and despite the near approach of Xmas,
no unusual festivity is apparent. Messrs. Wil-
liamson and Musgrove announce that they have
taken the Lyceum for a term of years, and will
commence business about the 24th, but the public
still yearns after full particulars and gets them
not. Also, the management of the Royal, at the
time of going to press, continues to maintain a
dead silence that can be heard for miles.
** * *
When the trustees of N.S.W. National
Art Gallery decided to spend a certain sum of
money in the purchase of strictly Australian
works by Australian artists, it was recognised to
be a step in the right direction. It was an
encouragement to our painters to leave the beaten
track of conventionalism and to produce works
representative of local life and manners. But,
good as the idea was in its conception, the way in
which it has been carried out up to now shows
that it is predestined to failure. No better proof
of this could be found than in the result of the
competition just concluded. The trustees in their
prospectus announced that twelve of the pictures
sent in would be bought, provided, of course, that
so many were up to standard ; but nevertheless
the judges have decided to purchase only five of
the drawings. There would be, perhaps, no great
cause for complaint on this score had the choice
made been judicious, but we submit that anyone
with the slightest knowledge of the subject can, on
viewing the show, see at a glance that such is not
the case. * J. R. Ashton’s “ Terrigal Headland”
should undoubtedly have been bought, as it is
beyond question the best work of his the writer
has ever seen. The wave-drawing is particularly
good, and the only fault one can find with the
picture is that the cloud resting on the
horizon is lacking in gradation. W. L. Lister’s
view of the entrance to Port Stephens,,
with its cleverly treated sand - bank in the
foreground, has also fine qualities, but in many
respects it is inferior to both his “Page Valley”
and his “Myall River,” also exhibited. Of the
other three purchases it is not possible to speak,
except in terms of the strongest condemnation. J.
S. Diston’s “ Broken Hill ’’ (an unpromising sub-
ject), is nut without merit, but its monotonous
colour and amateurishly-painted sky render it
unworthy of a place in any public gallery : while
Mr. E. B. Boulton’s mountain scene is simply a
carefully stippled drawing of the Birket Foster
school, utterly lacking in strength. The best that
can be said of A. J. Fischer’s “Cradling in
Golden Gully ” is that it is superior to any of the
previous productions of the same artist. There
are good points in the drawing, as, for example, in
the treatment of the river-bed and the distant
hills, but the figures are ill-drawn, and altogether
the work is quite below the standard which
should entitle it to a permanent place in a
national collection of works of art. But when one
turns from the pictures mentioned and con fern -

choose, one’s astonishment at their decision is
increased fourfold. Streeton’s scholarly drawing
of the “Valley of the Mittagong” is beyond ques-
tion the finest landscape in the whole collection.
Then, again, A. H. Full wood has surpassed him-
self in his “ Mitchell’s Bridge, Victoria Bass,” in
which, by the way, the work is advantageously

artist. E. P. Mahoney’s drawing of a bullock-

distinct advance on any of his previous efforts in -
the same medium, but the subject is thoroughly
characteristic of bush-life; and Mr. Minus’s land-
scapes,, with their delicate warm greys, and the
excellent treatment of the skies, should certainly
have received more consideration at the hands of
the judges. The moral of these competitions,
good as they are in intention, seems to be that,
while the decision is left to persons who appear
crassly ignorant, the more competent local artists
will prefer to take their chance iu the open market
to running the risk of having their talents mis-
judged.

Max O’Rell has been taken by R. S.
Smythe to Riverina, which must have been
very familiar ground to the Much-Travelled. It
was at Deniliquin, 33 years ago, that he edited
a weekly squatting newspaper, receiving _£l2 a
week, probably the highest salary ever paid to a
country editor. What changes the old wanderer
must have noticed since his time, when the jovial
John Kelly was police - magistrate, and the
Desaillys owned Coree, and D. G. J ones ran the
Pastoral Times, and Tom Robertson was pound-
keeper, and the genial Dr. Morrison kept open
house, and the veteran Dr. Noyes was a nevv-
churn, and the Royal Hotel was a wooden shanty
where overlanders drank champagne, and Nebu-
chadnezzar Tyson had a run on the north bank of
the Edward, unmolested by free selectors ! And
Deniliquin still boasts of its “ culchaw. The
residents turned out in evening-dress at Max
ORell’s show, nearly everybody went into the
front seats, and the ladies made Smythe promise
that Max should give them another night when he
came back from Maoriland.
** * *
One week’s run sufficed to exhaust “ A
White Lie ”at Melbourne Bijou. The burden of
an unintelligent child was more than its weak
back-bone could boar, and the unsolved doubt as
to what really became of the evil dude was a mill-
stone round its neck. The play provtd nothing
except the absurdity of a married woman falling
in love with another fellow when her husband is
snoozing on the sofa hard by, and certain to wake
up presently. Moreover, one grows tired of the
stage lady who doesn’t take ordinary precautions
to prevent these unpleasantnesses. She is ag in
nature, and any rational husband would east her
off for ever, for being a fool. On Saturday, the
farewell fortnight of B. & B. was inaugurated
with a revival of “The Solicitor,” or the
“Misery of a Hansom Cab.” Brough repeated
his indiscretion of driving a cab for a wager, and
Mrs. Brough engaged the vehicle under suspicious
circumstances as of old. Two giddy men in
buckram repeated their former endeavours to
trifle with the fresh young affections of the cab-
man’s daughter, and an all-round discovery of every-
body else in a secret hiding-place occurred once
again. “ The Solicitor ” is worth seeing at any
time. Now that the Bijou company are about to
leave their old haunt for an indefinite period, it
behoves Melbourne folks to hurry up and be there
for the sake of auld lang syne. They laugh best
who laugh last, and the nights for laughing at B.
and B.’s farcical comedies are numbered and
ticketed. Next Saturday (10th) the menu will be
changed again to “ A Village Priest. ’

the one and only Titheradge takes his benefit on

Likewise, the Gaiety Co., in their present
combination, are about to bid Melbourn) adieu.
Individual members will presently appear in
divers pantomimes, but as exponents of the legiti-
mate leg-drama in Australia they have almost
finished their engagement. “Miss Esmeralda”
is announced to end on Saturday, and a painful
rumour that the Princess’ will close its doors for
the next fortnight is in the air. In which case,
the city will be without a theatre where the weary
scribe can rest his legs during the week before
Christmas. Meanwhile, “ Miss Euneralda ”
spells big business, and Lonnen’s Australian edi-
tion of “Mrs. ’Enry ’Awkins”has touched the
gods on a tender spot. Poor old Nellie Farren is
much missed in the leading “ boy part, but the
general effect is good enough. Also, the staging
is consistently gorgeous, and the young men in
the circle resemble one another in their beauty,
like so many green peas in a pod.
#* * *
The music-halls are doing their best to
tide over the time of trouble which always comes
to shows just before Xmas. The Gaiety is open
as usual and the Coghill Bros, have put fortli a
strong variety bill at St. George’s where the
maximum price is a modest lt is here
that Will Whitburn takes the shine out of
Lonnen’s larrikin song, Whitburn being in-
digenous to the soil and having considered the
lilies of the “push” how they grow, from his
youth upwards. But the study of larrikins and
costers has gone far enough, one fancies. A
“comic” ditty purveyor possessed of a taste for
impersonating gentlemen would be a genuine
novelty. It is desirable, too, that Melbourne
audiences should get a notion of what a gentle-
man, expensively dressed or otherwise, looks like
on a platform.

At Poverty Point.
At Melbourne Princess, on a recent night, the chatter
nuisance in the circle was very pronounced for some time,
but was promptly suppressed by a man who knew neither
fear nor Mrs. Grundy. To the friends between whom he
sat he simply said, “ I’ll stop those chattering fools. You
fellows prepare to look unconscious. They did their
best, and just as the cackle was again becoming painful,
the centre man remarked, in a voice that permeated the
whole theatre like a cold shiver, “ D nit all, give the
actor a show, can’t you?” For the rest of that evening
you could have cut the silence with a hunk of hoopHion.
Which recalls a parallel case at the Opera House. The
offenders were a showy society woman and a certain aide-
de-camp. She was another man’s wife, and ho had come
chiefly to spoon her, such being his playful habit. 1 re-
gently when theirvoiees rose to toohigb a whisper, someone
behind said, “ Hush, there! ” He scowled, she pursed
her lips, neither ceased. Then came another loudei
“ Hush, there ! ” Still the murmur went on. Then a
voice as of thunder, “ Look here, you two, shut up, oi go
home ! ” They were silent, and at the end of the first act
a hansom received them, and they passed off into outei
darkness.
Arthur Garner again appears as a polite letter-writer—
this time in London. (His last victim was Jimmy
Thompson, of Melbourne Standard.) Arthur, who has
had a law-suit on with a certain Londoner, well known in
Australia, sends this (o his opponent’s solicitor:— Sir,—
Mr. has informed me of the statement you made re-
garding my character on Monday last whilst applying for
a further postponement of the case ‘ v. '• Lpon
meetino- your client later in the day, I questioned him as
to your'authority for such statement, and after .saying,
(i stly, that he knew nothing about it, he wound up hy
declinin')* to give any information on the subject. I then
told him emphatically that whoever is responsible is a
liar. I now repeat this assertion to you, with the timber
comment that if you took upon yourself to tell the he on
vour own responsibility, the cowardice of the proceeding
is accentuated by the fact of your being sheltered profes-
sionally from tiie pu lishment such conduct deserves.
Thiwhout this vexatious action (now entered upon its
seeond year) 1 have endeavoured to fight fairly, and my
instructions to my solicitors have a’ways been to extend
all courtesy to the opposite side, and 1 have never
descended to the contemptible trick of making lying
statements as to the personal character of my opponent.
Two wrongs does not make on ; right, so I shall continue
the same line of conduct hitherto adopted by me during
this action, though probably neither you nor your client
can appreciate or understand the instinctive honour that
guides and controls the behaviour of an English gentle-
man.—Yours truly, Arthur Garner.”
Herbert Flemming, writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent by last mail, is out of the bill at the Haj-
market, but Mrs. Langtry lias offered him an engagement
at £l5 per week to play in a curtain-raiser. Herbert
hesitates to accept, and thinks it somewhat infra dig. to
take part in a front piece. He is putting on flesh rapidly,
and is anything but an ideal lover in looks. He is, how-
ever, considered to be a coming man.

about Australia, said to have been made in Ceylon, with
what he says in London. “Here,” writes The Bulletin
correspondent, “he is enthusiastic in praise of the Land of
the Kangaroo. There must lie two Standings, as there
were two Philips of Macedon.”

A former Melbourne conductor once said to Woodfleld
at a “ Chiiperic ” dress rehearsal: “ When will you learn
to sing?” The vocalist didn’t say when, but shook a
whip fn the amorous one’s face, with the remark, “ If you
abuse me before the chorus, I’ll thrash you. Theic was
no more abuse that season.

Garnet Walch’s drama, “ Silver Chimes,” has pleased
them in Adelaide. The title smacks of the G. R. Sims
article. Extract of “ Silver Falls and Bells of
Haslemere.”
When President Zeal bought the dress-circle at Mel-
bourne Princess’s for his Parliamentary night he also
hired the adjacent bar for self and friends only. The
outside public were refused admission to licensed premises
on this gorgeous occasion, an arrangement which,
although presumably an outrage against the law', pre-
vented the vulgar herd from discovering how many mem-
bers of the Senate were taking too much to drink. Poor
creatures in the amphitheatre were able to estimate the
consumption of free liquor by the dress-circle’s “ breff,’
which floated upwards along with the other hot air.
Had man not been essentially a polygamous animal,
what would have become of the comic drama?
That naughty verse of one of the Gaiety songs has not
appeared in “Esmeralda” since the first night, and great
is the disappointment thereof.

by degrees and beautifully more.

Lily Langtry is not “mealy-mouthed” about her age,
and owns up to being 40. She does not look more than
30 or 32, and can well afford to give away ten years to
ordinary women.

Poor David Lee, Melbourne’s civic organ-grinder, has
been practically extinguished by Ernest Wood, of the
Anglican Cathedral. Wood’s recitals and service per-
formances attract the class of “city men” who, in days
of yore, found themselves strolling into church to hear
Moorhouse preach.

invitation to Melbourne Princess’s affect to have been

It has been found that 32 pianists, simultaneously play-
ing a duet upon 16 pianos in unison, produce, in a big
hall, no fuller tone than that of a first-rate pianist upon a
single piano. Thus is annihilated the time-honoured
riddle: Q., What makes more noise than a pig tied to a
gatepost and being whacked ? Ans., Two pigs tied to two
gateposts, and both being whacked.
Clement Scott never takes a note.

tion, Christmas.

chere), as well as to Georgina Hodson (Mrs. Lyster).
Hambleton, one of the first Melbourne comedians (he
dated from much before 1850) was a bush preacher up to
lately.
Out in West Queensland the local amateurs were wrest-
ling with the “Octoroon.” When the ship-burning scene
came on the blue fire couldn’t be found at the opportune
moment, and the audience had to imagine the appalling
sight. Later in the evening, when the apotheosis of Zoe
was taking place she was wafted skywards accompanied
by a livid streak of blue flame. The amateur property man
had it in his pocket and thoughtlessly placed his pipe
amongst it.
Doddery Toole lately went to a butcher’s, with
“ Families Supplied,” and asked for a boy and a girl.
The “ Duchess of Haiti,” just acted at the Independent
Theatre, London, is the cheerfulest thing in the English
language. The Duchess holds cut her hand to grab that
of her lover, and, instead of that, the jealous Duke slaps
into her hand a dead man’s one, and shows her wax
figures, as the bodies of the lover and their three illegiti-
mate children, after which the Duchess is nigh tortured
to death by a swarm of mad women let loose on her, and
then she is strangled before the audience.
Bruiser Corbett is going on the stage with a new drama
which seems to be written for the express purpose of
goading that miserable tough, Charley Mitchell, on to
single combat. Mitchell is the bad character of the
drama—a beery, dropsical rowdy, who is always drunk,
and always offering to lick anyone in the blessed
country. At last he insults the memory of Corbett’s
mother, and that gentleman welts him to such an extent
that he becomes practically unrecognisable. If Mitchell
ever sees this play the betting is that he will rush the
stage and start laying the company out with a chair-leg.
The rage for strong men still continues in London.
Sandow’s latest performance is with a pair of dumb-bells
Bft. long. When he has gone thro igh the usual gyrations
with them, they are unscrewed, and a couple of men step
out. Also, Sandow’s assistant gets up on a trapeze near
the roof, and drops three sacks of corn on to the athlete’s
neck, and when that useless mass of flesh has caught
them successfully, there is a howl of applause which
makes the Trocadero shake to its foundations.

Dear Bulletin, —The licensee of an Aus-
tralian city public-house does not always keep his
hat on when serving, to distinguish him from his
barman. He very often takes off his hat and
tucks up his shirt-sleeves in order that he may be
taken for a barman by casual customers. Useful
wrinkles and gems of information are thereby
gleaned—and he knows who are Hebe’s best
friends. —Yours, Old Dromore.
Victor Houghton, who suicided at Coogee
the other day, after writing so cool a letter to the
Coroner, had a chequered career. Educated in
Germany (the home of suicide), he was a N.S.W.
O.S. surveyor for some years, came into money
and married money, knocked it all down racing
and mining, and separated from his wife. He was
famous during one whole day, having ridden over
the big jumps at Randwick without mistake, for a
considerable wager.
Edward O’Donoghue, the “ silenced ”
priest, a very well-known, clerically - garbed
character in Melbourne, who got six months for
vagrancy last week, was known to many as
“Teddy Royce,” which pleasant person he re-
sembled facially to a surprising degree. O’Dono-
ghue had capped a lengthy cadging career by
striking a well-known medico for 12s. (id., and
then crossing the road to another with the same
yarn about wanting a fare to Sydney for a poor
man. Medico No. 1 followed him, gave him
away, and then gave him in charge. In court,
O’Donoghue informed the Bench that he “got
.£lOO a year from Le Temps, Freeman’s Journal
and Cork Examiner, by writing the usual letter;’
also, that he had property in Brisbane.
There are in England about 14,000 parsons
of the Established Church, and of these last year
375 were found guilty of various offences. Four-
teen were in trouble for breach of promise ; 18 for
cruelty to animals; 17 for elopement (7 of these went
off with someone else’s wife, and the rest mostly
went off with a female under age and neglected
to marry her); 12 suicided ; 121 were arrested for
drunkenness ; 109 for assault; and the rest were
charged with seduction, perjury, obtaining money
under false pretences, unpublishable crimes, and
miscellaneous offences. In England the average
number of convictions is 2GG out of every 100,000
population. Therefore, the parsons of the Estab-
lished Church are just about 12 times more
criminal and more beastly, on an average, than
the rest of the community. And yet people want
to know why clerical influence is on the decline
and people don’t respect the Church,
According to N.Y. Recorder, there are
at least two suicide-clubs iu the States, one in
Chicago, the other in Philadelphia. One J. Bar-
low Morehead suicided iu the latter city on
Monday, Oct. 3, “as per club.” The club in
Philadelphia consists of 17 members, and meets
once a year, on Good Friday, to elect a president.
On taking the oath of office, he vows to kill him-
self within a year by shooting, time and place
being left to his own discretion. There is also
a clause in the rules providing that some other
member may be told off for suicide in addition
to the president. Another queer club in New
York is “The Last Man Club.” Founded about
ten years ago, it originally consisted of thirteen
members. Once a year since then the club has
met. At the table are thirteen seats, with plates
for thirteen diners, and overhead is a chandelier
with thirteen jets. The wine is drunk from
human skulls to uncanny toasts. At the opening
of the feast the president calls the original roll.
Originally every member swore to be present at
every feast —in body, if alive; in spirit, if dead.
Therefore, although, for each unanswered name,
the president turns out one light, a dinner is
served all the same before each vacant chair, the
spirit of the departed being supposed to be seated
in his place. There will probably come a time
when at that annual feast there will be but one
living person. On that chandelier only one light
will be burning. In the twelve vacant chairs
will be twelve ghosts. That “Last Man” will
suicide that night, and the next meeting of the
club will be in the fields of asphodel.
BILL ANDREWS’ CHUM.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A seedy wreck is my actor friend
With his pose of studied grace,
And the nasal blossoms that softly blend
With the grease-paint on his face.
And bis grating chuckles that ever seem
Like the grit of a coffee-mill,
And his doleful tales of the old rigime,
And the days when he played with “ Bill.”
You talk of Anson and Boucioauit,
And Brough, why, I’d swap them all
To see Ruth Oakley on the show
With Rayner playing Paul ;
And “ Tiddywinks ” Collins and Russell, too,
And Dicky Stewart and Hill,
Bill Andrews—why, where’s your actor now
That can “ cackle ” a part like “ Bill ? ”
He’d wander on through the Thespian maze
Till the beer made his voice grow thick,
’Mid the shadowy scenes of the palmy days
On the boards of the “ good old Vic.”
To vary the subject I’d try in vain.
For, in spite of my utmost skill,
He’d wrestle away and begin again,
On the days when he played with “ Bill.”
Poor fellow, he once was respected and rich,
Now he plays “ The carriage waits,”
And says that they always “queer his pitch ”
As he’s “ multy on his plates ; ”
But he’ll drink to the joy of other days,
Let the pain come now if it will,
Till the Prompter Death rings dow n the baize
On his happy dreams of “ Bill.”

He borrowed half — i.e., gave bills for a million
and discounted them for £914,708, or £9l 9s. sd.
for every hundred. Why didn’t the great man

Shemarbonee !
Matvieff, the popular superintendent of
Queensland telegraphs, has returned from a trip
to Europe. He is a boy of the old school. An
operator once urged as a reason for promotion
that he didn’t drink. “You don’t drink, eh ! ”
said old Matvieff, “I suppose it’s because you’re
too d mean.”
Mr. “Jones,” the Melb. hangman, when
he goes up-country to do a “job,” is usually in
charge of a warder in plain clothes. “When I
went to Ballarat last I was hooted,” said Jones
lately. “But the beggars couldn’t have known
which was me only for that damned bag that I
carry the rope in.”

They are telling a gruesome tale in Bris-
bane about a saint who once went to comfort a
condemned man in his cell. He told the miserable
wretch all about Gehenna, and then finished by
taking up a collection, and when the congregation,
in a cold sweat of horror, bequeathed his only
mundane possession, adirty£snote, to the “caws,”
the good man took it and departed in peace.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent:
It may do a good turn to someone if I state that
the sum of £667 9s. 3d. awaits claims from the
heirs of William Stuart Scott, “ late of Australia,”
at the Cape. The Curator of Intestate Estates at
Cape Town is the person to apply to. Strict proof
of heirship is required, but no unnecessary official
routine stands in the way of those entitled to it
getting the money.
The “image” of Governor Norman exists
in the person of a Brisbane cabby. The likeness
is accentuated by the fact that both dress most
unpretentiously, the former from choice, the
latter, perhaps, from necessity. More than one
near-sighted dame has given the jarvie a sweeping
bow, in mistake. One went so far as to enquire
after “ her dear Ladyship and Miss Grace” before
discovering the error.
Father Kernen, defendant in the late
Walsh will-case in Melbourne, was fellow-priest
at South Sydenham, London, with Rev. Addis,
now assistant to Dr. Strong at the Australian
Church. Addis “changed his views,” married
the lady sacristan, and came to Australia.
Kernen remained a celibate, but came to Aus-
tralia also, and settled down in Collingwood,
close to the gilded Walsh.
Mysterious hints of “surprises” and
“ unexpected light,” in store for the public at the
hearing of the Speight v. Syme action, are finding
their way into Age paragraphs. The Age is evi-
dently getting frightened. On the other hand,
Speight is saying nothing about the light that he
intends to shed upon certain political arrange-
ments. Meanwhile, they are betting 2 to 1 in
Melbourne against the chance of the cause celhbre
ever coming into court.
Sir Saul Samuel, N.S.W. Agent-General,
has in his possession a book with the inscription :
“To the Right Honourable W. E. Gladstone,
with the respectful compliments of Henry Parkes”
—given him by a man who picked it up in a book-
shop for a couple of shillings. Evidently the
G.O.M. doesn’t think much of his shoddy
“colonial” imitator, or he wouldn’t have dis-
carded the presentation, which, however, perhaps
didn’t cost Parkes much, as it bears the Govern-
ment imprint. Which reminds one that Marmier,
one of the French “ Immortals,” in rummaging at
a city bookstall, came across one of his own works
which he had presented to a literary confrere. He
bought the book, got it most expensively bound,
and sent it back to his friend, with a note saying
that he hoped he would keep it this time for the
sake of the binding.
Sir Edwin T. Smith (S.A.), who made his
triple Mayoralty of Adelaide, and his parlia-
mentary position, and his bauble, out of Beer, is
about to retire from politics and go travelling.
His constituents are getting tired, he hears the
roar of the political deluge, and is going in out of
the wet. He was once a cellarman; became an
Adelaide brewer’s drummer; then a junior
partner ; finally blossomed into the Bismarck of a
brewery. The Smith, as a politician, has no more
brains than a plaster-of-paris image ; but he has
got along by the amiability of his character, and
the immense profits from “tied ” and other public-
houses. In public opinion, he is at least a semi-
millionaire.
While on circuit to Roma (Q.) lately,
Judge Paul inadvertently left behind him, at
Dalby, his official wig and robes. But Geo.
Byrne, who was prosecuting for the Crown, had
not been so remiss, and, at the Judge’s request,
lent him his own paraphernalia. When the court
opened, barrister Byrne rose to make an apology :
“ Your Honor, I wish to crave your indulgence.
The weather is so hot that I have felt obliged to
appear before you without my wig and gown.
Will you excuse me?” “Certainly,” said Paul,
without the flicker of a smile; “ I should have
done the same as you under the circumstances.
What is the first case?”
The latest portrait of Her Gracious on
English post-card stamps is a characteristic
fiction. The Queen is here represented as a tall,
slim, graceful widow of comparatively tender
years, holding her fan in a eoquettishly regal
style. The most grovelling of illustrated papers
never conceived so flagrant a violation of the
truth as the post-card stamp designer was guilty
of. Victoria Regina happens to be a thick set,
old lady, with a specially large head, standing
sft. Sin. in low-heeled shoes, and weighing some-
where between 12 and 15 stone, and if loyalty to
the Throne requires feeding with attractive pic-
tures of an imaginary monarch, it must be a sickly
sentiment indeed.
New setting of an old fable : “ Dives fares
sumptuously every day on the crumbs purloined
from the wallet of Lazarus.”— Thorold Rogers.

Alfred Joseph, the cheery, moon-faced
Bendigo metallician, has made a half-crown book
entitled “A Bendigonian Abroad,” about his
travels in foreign lands. It is somewhat curious
that no one has ever heard Mr. Joseph talk in the
Victoria Club or the betting-paddock in the chatty
yet superior manner observed in his book, wherein
he insinuates himself as a judge of the cuisine, of
cigars, of wines, of opera, of painting, of sculpture,
of politeness, of couriers, of cremation, and of
music, besides evincing an intimate knowledge of
modern and ancient history, about which he is
able to patter familiarly as one in whose grasp it
is all nutshelled. Unlike his immediate author-
predecessor (Nehemiah Bartley), Alf. doesn’t
seem to have an all-approving eye for lovely
woman, but gives his readers the idea of a
man who, when in foreign parts, ever sighs
for a slice of underdone Australian beef. Alfred,
we regret to notice, holds Monarehial views,
and strongly disapproves of Republicanism
which reminds us that we have yet to meet a
Republican bookmaker. Space permits not of
our following the distinguished author in his
wanderings, save perhaps to note that in the
States he rooted out a Mormon with four wives
and 17 brats, and that at Honolulu, on the road
home to Bendigo to skin the lamb, he was dis-
gusted to find that even the red seal of the Ballarat
Corporation, held by a fellow traveller, was power-
less to obtain an interview with the dusky queen.
Thus do Alfred’s most sacred feelings well up in
peroration :
What feelings of joy fill our hearts ! Home,
home ! Once more to see our dfar ones, and the many
friends for whom our affection has been but intensified
by absence. ... As we try to sleep in the Sydney
express train, which is flying towards Melbourne, silently
we offer a prayer of gratitude to the Great King of Kings
for His bounteous mercy
“Ten to one bar one !” is shouted right under-
neath the reviewer’s window, and he therefore
can’t pray with Joseph.
The Great Race.
The race for the Melbourne Cup of 1892 lias
come and gone, and the anticipations of thou-
sands of people for a year have disappeared in
something under the space of four minutes—some
are richer (and more poorer) by those four
minutes’ experience, in which tens of thousands
felt an intoxication something akin to the in-
fluence of wine. There is a greater race than
even this enormous Cup event. From birth until
old age man is engaged in a contest with Death ;
and only those who are swifter than the Reaper
stay above ground for any length of time. Have
you never felt as a young man the pride of simply
being? You experience it most on a spring morn-
ing, when you enjoy the true health of absolute
unconsciousness as to the possession of heart, lungs,
kidneys, liver, or any digestive apparatus at all.
You are almost strong enough to walk indefinitely,
or you feel that a push of your powerful shoulder
could force down a wall. As for Death, you laugh
at him ; you snap your lingers in his face and
defy his power : and by-and-bye you distance him,
and he remains a crest-fallen laggard, miles away
in your rear. But there comes a day when your
step is slow and your eyes are dimned, and Death,
aided by Disease —who is one of his progenitors
catches you again, and repays you all the insults
and sneers you gave him in the days of your
strength while he races neck and neck with you
for the goal. And in these days of unnatural
business excitement, and rapid living and think-
ing, the greatest aid of the dreaded dark horse,
who is galloping so steadily and so silently, is
that most common scourge, kidney disease. Uric
acid poison is the most fruitful cause of disease.
Bright’s disease, rheumatism, gout, gravel, stone,
and numerous other complaints are directly
traceable to its presence in the blood. The only
medicines so far known having any direct and
permanent curative effect on these diseases are
Warner’s Safe Cure and Bills. They have proved
their efficacy beyond cavil, while additional sub-
stantiation of their wonderful influence is coming
in day by day.

POLITICAL POINTS
Friends of Bible-reading in State schools
are busy hunting up the S.A. electors.
Paradox from a S.A. gubernatorial speech:
“The Governor of a .self-governing colony,” &c.
The Age now derides the Shiels Govern-
ment, and calls it “the Considering Ministry.”
N.S.W, Upper House begins its slaughter
by throwing out Walker’s Reduction of Costs of
Litigation Bill.
It is averred that Ewing, M.P., is getting
ready for a merciless Parliamentary onslaught
upon Jawbone Neild.
Brisbane Courier proposes the enquiry
“whether, and to what extent, wage-slavery
obtains in our institutions.”
Stradbroke Island, Moreton Bay, has been
proclaimed a leper lazaret. The ghastly list of
lepers lengthens every week.
Political rumours have it that the relations
between N.S.W. Premier Dibbs and “Works”
Lyne are those of rats and phosphorus.
In N.S.W. the example of Maoriland is
now being followed, union-meetings (in many
instances) being held with closed doors.
Horse-doctor Stewart, M.L.C., openly
says that the N.S.W. Ministry desire the defeat
of the Elections Act in the Upper House.
Last season the S.A. harvest averaged
about 4 bushels ; this season, is confidently
expected, with an exportable surplus of 314,002
tons.
A Victorian politician, sued for a doctor’s
bill, pleads as a set-off that he got the sawbones
the billet of J.P. Which means practically
that
Fitchett rightly remarks that should Sir
Charles Lilley “ return to politics, and lead the
Labour-party, politics in Queensland will grow
electrical! ”
A nice Government representative, Salo-
mons. His Upper House speech in favour of the
N.S.W. Electoral Bill was mere damnation by
faint praise.
As The Bulletin long ago pointed out,
one of the dodges of the N.S.W. squatter is to
get country, liable to selection, proclaimed
“ forest-reserves.”
Apropos Womanhood Suffrage in the Isle
of Man. It is explained that the right is guarded
by a property qualification and is confined to
spinsters and widows.
The Victorian public wanted a Protec-
tionist Government, but when Shiels starts pro-
tecting Matthew Davies, they begin to regard it
as M'Kinleyism run mad.
Somebody should lay on the table of the
Vic. Assembly a full list of members who are too
hopelessly insolvent to be able to go straight,
commencing with—but no matter.

responsible for all the trouble that has occurred in
N.S.W.” Not all. They aren’t responsible for
the existence of John Hornsby Davies.
Premier Downer (S.A.) protests that he
lias nothing whatever to do with the National
Defence League or its policy. He claims to be a
Liberal with a trend towards Radicalism.

>Ome, says that the revival of the Kanaka slave-
trade has created the impression in England that
Queensland is not a white man’s country.
The Governor of British New Guinea com-
plains of the inability of the natives to comprehend
the alienation of their land in perpetuity. They
hold that the chiefs can only sell a life-interest.

upon scores of Chinamen can go backwards and
forwards between N.S. Wales and Victoria, at

last week on private members’ motions in N.S.W.

The Code Napoleon is comprised in a
small volume which can easily be carried in the
waistcoat pocket. Griffith’s statutes alone fill
four huge volumes, to say nothing of such cherished
relics as the statutes of George IV. which Queens-
land took over at Separation.
One of the most melancholy sights ever
witnessed in N.S.W. Legislative Council was sup-
plied last week, when old Sir William Manning
stood up to dodder about the ghastly iniquity of
establishing true manhood suffrage, and to moon-
ingly plead the claims of a vote for “ thrift.”
Jacob, nominee M.L.C. (N.S.W.), says
“a seaman’s vote can be bought for a glass of
grog.” The birthrights of the world’s Esaus
have ever been bought for a mess of pottage.
But the Jacobs of the world should be content
with chiselling Esau. They needn’t “ rub it in.” *

vised Colonial Secretary Tozer that an aboriginal
who owns a 16-perch freehold is entitled to have
his name placed on the electoral-roll for the dis-
trict; while a half-caste standson the same footing
as a white. The next squatting game will be to
bestow titles of simh freeholds upon native niggers
and thus nobble the new votes. As for the half-
castes, they will ail naturally vote for their
“faders.”

A certain N.S. Wales M.L.C. believes in
“a vote for thrift.” Of course he does. He is
credited with having, when a Minister of the
Crown with .£l5OO a year, lived on 30s. a week
for years. Very likely he’s still just as miserable
as ever. So far as “ thrift ” goes, he is entitled to
50 extra votes.

of N.S.W. population, has 655 miles of railway
open and 545 under construction; total, 1200
miles. N.S.W. has 2266 miles open, and 252
under construction ; total, 2518 miles. At the
end of ’9l W.A.’s population was 53,285; its
debt, £1,514,269, or £2B Bs. 4d. per head.

Trickett, M.L.C. (N.S.W.) has an Ameri-
can friend with whom he was talking about vote-
registration. Said the Yank : “ Well, we can
get over that over there. We can generally turn
an election because we buy up 400 or 500 of these
registration-tickets. Of course we do not use
them, but still it prevents the other side trom
getting them.”
Griffith’s pet Constitution Bill won’t satisfy
the North Queenslanders. As for 20 years they
have agitated tor Home Rule, mere provincial
separation won’t satisfy them. In fact strong
pressure is being brought to bear on the new
Secretary of State, and it is probable that the
Kanakalanders will have autonomous control ot
N.Q. next year.
Agent-General Bray (he has been hyphen-
ated and is now known in England as Oox-Bray),
finding himself small potatoes in London, wants to
return to Adelaide; but the politicians who gave
him the post to get him out of the way will do'
nothing to relieve him of his duties. r lhey are
even prepared to be deaf to the inevitable plea,
when it comes along, that Lady Bray is losing her
health.
Macdonald’s demise took the kingbolt out
of Canadian loyalty. Only a day or two ago,
Montreal was stirred by a great public meeting,
which declared for Canadian Independence, while
U.S. Annexation came second, the status quo vyas
comparatively nowhere, and Imperial P ederation
nowherer. The Montreal Herald, the chier
local paper, persistently advocates Canadian
Independence.
The Foreign-traders of N.S.W. are per-
petually urging other people to go and settle on
the land, rather than attempt to create manu-
factures by means of Protection. Yet either the
Foreign-traders don’t settle on the land, or else
when they do they straightway abandon their
principles, for the N.S.W. agricultural districts
are the stronghold of Protection. What is the
meaning of this?
Pilcher, Q.C. (N.S.W.), “ cannot under-
stand the man who, by thrift and honest industry,
has acquired a little property for his family,
agreeing to a man who is here to-day and away to-
morrow, having exactly the same voice as himself
in legislating for the rights of property, and for
all the rights that men hold dear.” Meaning
thereby, that Pilcher, the over-protected lawyer,
can’t understand anyone being unselfish.

THE USE OF COLONIAL GOVERNORS.
1ST. OLD MAID: “I call Tabby darling, ‘Carrington’ because he
has such a neat moustache.”
2ND. DITTO: “I call my beauty, ‘Jersey’; he’s such a lovely
tortoise-shell.”
N.Q. Herald designates Sam’s cablegram “ a
shamefully dishonest one—a suppression of the
truth which is as utterly immoral as a deliberate
falsification of the facts would be.” The trick of
the political welsher.
A N.S.W. member of Parliament the
other day obtained the loan of a Government
steam-launch for a private picnic. While he had
the launch in use, the Government had to hire
another in its stead, for official work. Why,
when the member applied, didn’t the Treasurer
simply take £5 or £lO out of the Treasury till
and hand it to the applicant with— “ There you
are, dear boy ! go and buy yourself a steam-launch
for the day?” It would have amounted to the
same thing, and would have been a much simpler
and more candid operation.
A bitter and extremely bad-tempered
attack on the Melbourne Trades and Labour
Council, from the pen of Rudyard Kipling,
appeared in a recent Age. Nobody expects a red-
haired Jingo like Kipling to have any sympathy
with men who are dissatisfied with the Flag of
England, and other conventional rags and frippery
in which his idols are clothed. This young man
misunderstands his own drift. As a private indi-
vidual, he is an intense lover of constituted
authority and the mighty “ Isas a writer—
when not writing to order—unconsciously he
voices the sullen protestations of the herd against
privilege, usurpation, and tyranny.
The Australasian, in noticing a recent
very high shearing tally, argues from the
circumstance that shearers are too well paid.
The Australasian says nothing of the man
who only does his 30 sheep daily and who
would be absolutely starved out if the price per
hundred were reduced. Eighty per day per man
is a high average in any shed ; sixty, probably, is
nearer the average mark. But the Australasian
should be the last to denounce the high returns of
superior ability. Thus arguing, it reduces itself
below the standard of the “levellers” that it so
heartily condemns. The only valid argument for
individualism (it’s a subterfuge) is that it recog-
nises and recompenses superior ability.
If the Ballance (M.L.) Cabinet falls at
next election, it will be through the hostility of
the shop-keeping class. The Maoriland Govern-
ment has enacted that shop-assistants—about the
most hard-driven and underpaid class in the com-
munity—are in future to enjoy one half-holiday
per week, and now every grocer and bootmaker
from the North Gape to Stewart’s Island who
employs so much as one red-headed message-boy
has gone into opposition. Also, some of the ultra-
religious classes object to the innovation on the
ground that the Bible says : “ Six days shalt thou
labour,” and therefore the idea of labouring only
five and a-half days is “ unscreeptural,” and cal-
culated to bring down “ a judgment ” on the
country.
It is alleged against Premier Shiels, by
even his own Press patron (the Age) that “his
administration of the Attorney-General’s depart-
ment in the presence of the great banking scandals
which have demoralised the public conscience at
home, and damaged the fair name of the colony
abroad, has been certainly of the weakest, just
where weakness may be disaster. The part he
has played in refusing to appoint any Crown
officer to examine and report on alleged frauds
has been to undermine the respect for justice in
the public mind, and to apparently justify our
acrid critics in England in their assertions that
corruption runs through the whole body of the
state.” Clearly the time has arrived when Shiels
will have to throw his friends over, or be pushed
aside himself by the hand that made him.
AN ODE TO “BRITISH JUSTICE.”
“The spirit of British Justice”—five
words which will be met with in any three lines
of any speech by Jack Want, and in any 3| of any
speech by Parkes : —
It’s lauded by slave-swindling bankers,
By Shiels,
By Parkes, and the fee grabbing lawyer.
Loud peals
They ring out for the beauties of justice
Brand British,
And we writhe to our souls as they worship
Their fetish ;'
How many sleek scoundrels from skilly
It’s saved,
While the pauper’s black path to the gallows
It’s paved !
For that foul land has placed on this fair land
Her brand,
To save from Christ’s justice the strong one’s
’Curst band.
But w'eak ones and slaves would have justice
By rods ;
And the justice they want is not Britain’s,
But God’s.
There are various ways of conducting
public meetings. One—which is favoured by
King Hannell, of Newcastle, N.S.W. —has its
drawbacks. He puts the amendment, which is
an addition to the original motion, first; it is
carried. Then he puts the original motion, which
is naturally lost. Then he puts this lost motion
with the amendment tacked on. But he got home
all the same.
As the constant dropping of water will
wear away the hardest stone, so do the various
processes of life wear out the physical organism ;
every motion of a finger, every breath inhaled,
every thought conceived, and every word spoken
causes destruction of some of the complex
molecules which together form the human system.
This results in formation of a quantity of effete
matter called “ waste products,” which if retained
in the system give rise to various diseases. If
retained in the muscular tissue they cause rheu-
matism, gout, &c. ;in the lungs, consumption ; in
the blood, skin disease, boils, pimples, blotches,
and kidney disease?. The blood is purified and
impurities expelled by the various processes
of excretion, respiration, perspiration, micturation,
and defecation. Where these functions are not
properly performed such symptoms as headache,
nervousness, palpitation, vomiting, diarrhoea,
toothache, neuralgia, and constipation are
frequent, and must have immediate treatment, or
serious disease will undoubtedly set in. If the
first symptoms are attacked a cure is easy ; if
neglected a cure is sometimes impossible. If
Clements Tonic is faithfully used quick relief is
assured. This medicine quickly restores the
normal action to any weakened or impaired organ ;
it purifies and strengthens the blood, stimulates
the kidneys and liver, and relieves the bowels of
all impactions of faecal matter, and strengthens
the nervous system generally. Its value is proved
by such cases as that of J. S. M‘Arthur, Esq.,
Balmain, N.S.W., who writes:—“Feeling some-
what queer of late, I consulted a medical gentle-
man, who rather scared me by stating that my
case was a bad one. I was ordered to at once
knock off smoking, for one thing, and to be very
careful not to excite or overwork myself, &c.
Failing to obey any advice, and by not taking the
medicine ordered, nothing could stop me from
“topping my boom” and get through my last
watch, only to appear again in the shape of an old
albatross. ' Having strictly obeyed all orders, 1
could not discover any improvement, and at last
was advised to try Clements Tonic. After some
persuasion I did so, with the result—a new fellow
altogether, I have only used three bottles, and
shall never fail to carry a supply, and acquaint
my fellow shimnasters of the good I derived from
using Clements Tonic.”—l am, yours faithfully,
Jno. 8. M‘Arthur, Master C. & N. C. Co.’ s.s.
“ Fernmount.”

THE UNEMPLOYED AT BROKEN
HILL.
The Premier has received a communica-
tion from the Mayor of Broken-hill with
regard to the unemployed at that town. It
seems that the Mayor had written to the
I Railway Commissioners of South Australia
for free passes to enable the unemployed to
go to Petersburg, a town situated at the
junction of the main railways in Soulh
Australia. It is also the central point of the
northern agricultural district. The Railway
Commissioners recommended the Mayor to
communicate with the Premier of South
Australia on the subject. The Mayor there*
upon telegraphed to Sir George Dibbs about
the question. Sir George has sent a telegram
to Sir John Downer and ’told him thatrfie
hardly liked to make the request, but if the
South Australian Government would agree
to allow the unemployed to travel on the
South Australian railway at a reduced rate
to enable them to reach Petersburg, he (Sir
George) would authorise the police magis-
trate at Broken-hill to issue passes to those
of the men who desired to proceed to the
place and seek work. Sir George had not
last evening received a reply to this message.

Society
The latest Melbourne sensation was pro-
bably arranged by a clever pressman for the sixth
“ edeeshuns ”of the evening papers. First they
find the legs ; then the horror is allowed time to
properly soak into the public sensibilities. The
discovery of the arms follows, and the affair is
evidently to be “ continued in our next.”
* * #
At latest dates, Melbourne was anxiously
awaiting the discovery of a human head and
trunk, supposed to have been formerly connected
with the arms and legs now lying at the local
morgue. Also, a bevy of young reporters from
the Standard and Herald offices were on the
alert to prevent, if possible, the finding of both
these missing articles on the same day. Two
doses of gory horror have already been obtained
from one corpse, and even the “ great ” morning
dailies are praying silently that the balance of the
tragedy may be revealed in, at least, two pieces,
on separate occasions. In our mind’s eye,
Horatio, we see it announced, 18 months hence—
“ The Corpse Complete! Our own Special Cop
respondent stumbles across the Skull at Mid-
night.”
When “Billy” Smyth, of Sydney Customs,
was en tour on leave, he visited Balmoral and
prowled round the lodge entrance in the hope of
seeing Her Gracious. At last, he ventured to
ad Iress the red-headed potentate who was doing
se itry-go at the Castle gates: “Look here,
officer,” said William, “do you know whether I
shall be able to see Her Majesty the Queen pass
this way to-day ? ” “Noo, look-a-here,” said the
red-headed kiltie, “I have obsairved you prooling
aroond the premises a’ the morn, wi’ no vara guid
intent, I’ll warrant. So I tell ye to be off, and if
you’ll no be going, I’ll help you awa’ by
pitting an inch or twa o’ this skewer
intil ye.” “You bloodthirsty monster!”
roared Billy; “for two (Australian adjective)
pins, I’d swing you round by your (A. a.) kilt, and
bash your (A. a.) brains out against the (A. a.)
sentry-box.” Which so flurried the sentry that he
rang up the officer-of-the-guard, to whom the
wrathful William explained that, as a loyal sub-
ject, and a loyaller Customs-house officer, and as
a stranger from distant Australia, he was only
hovering around on the off-chance of getting a
sight of his Noble Queen. The officer expressed
his sorrow that the Queen wasn’t on tap that day,
and Billy wants to stuff his friends that the sentry
was taken off duty ! that the officer asked him to
have a drink, and that he had three!
* * *
Another and more recent Billy Smyth
story : He had just landed from his official gig at
Prince’s Stairs, Circular Quay, Sydney, when
there arrived at the steps a man-o’-war’s boat,
containing three naval officers in plain clothes.
Now, Billy had often been royally entertained
aboard these seadogs’ vessels, and his simple mind
utterly refuses to harbour any of the awful codes
of shore etiquette. So, directly he saw these
officers, one of whom was no less a personage than
His Excellency Admiral Lord Charles Scott, he
felt bound to extend shore hospitalities to them.
So he advanced cheerfully and, saluting his Nibs,
said : “ How do, Lord Charles? Will you join me
in a little refreshment?” The other two naval
officers lengthened their figureheads and changed
their masthead colours at this hideous act of
sacrilege on the part of the ingenuous William, but
the Admiral set things right at once by saying :
“ No, Mr. Smyth, I can’t take a drink, you know,
but I am sure B and S will ”
B and S being two slightly subor-
dinate naval swells. Billy, in afterwards narrat-
ing the incident, said, with a fine touch of
self-justification: “It wasn’t as if I was asking
him into a little back-slum pub., or a threepenny
bar; no, sir, I asked him into a first-rate hotel,
where they sold only sixpenny drinks !” Sweet
William!
* # #
Chief Justice Way went on circuit during
November and carefully carried his famous Pome-
ranian pup—-the gracious gift of Lady Kintore—
about with him in all his wanderings. It is stated
that it will shortly be gazetted as judge’s asso-
ciate.
# * *
At Burra (S.A.) railway station the other
day, an officious official bounced into Judge Way’s
carriage and ordered him to hand over the pup
hereinbeforementioned, to be sent in a separate
compartment. Whereupon, the Chief Justice
aforesaid handed over—his card ! and The
Bulletin blushes to state that the officious official
obsequiously apologised, instead of lifting the
pup by the scruff of its neck and dropping it into
the dog-truck.

told his awed audience that Comet Biela was

1ST. MELBOURNE MAN: “For Heaven’s sake lend us something; the bailiffs are in my
house!”
2ND. DITTO: “ Most extraordinary coincidence! They’re in mine, too!”
already lashingits tail at the earth’s whiskers, and
that Cornet Brooks was coming up behind with

My! how the collections for the Angel Gabriel
did tinkle on the plate ! Now the pious funker is

The depression. “Give us this day our

* # #
The most damning allegation against Pastor
Abbott, the principal in the newest religious
scandal at Fitzroy, Melb., is that he permitted
the lady who boarded at his Faith-healing Home,
Bethshan - and who used to perch on his knee and
kiss him before and after meals —to mend his
trousers. The wife who will tamely submit to
the indignity of seeing another woman mending
her hubby’s pants —for kindness —in her own
home has not yet arrived. Pastor Abbott in
proof of the purity of his motives pointed out that
he opposed the wishes of a deluded brother who
thought to compel sisters to go to bed and submit
to be anointed privately. “I said it was not
decent, and I opposed it. I desired that two or
three sisters should be appointed to take up the
work, and the gentleman referred to vehemently
opposed it.” Faith-healing has its drawbacks.
They used to pray “Deliver us from temptation ’
twice a day at Bethshan.

Sixteen more attorneys admitted in
N.S.W. How are they to live? Well, an honest
attorney’s-sign would be the three balls of Lom-
bardy.

When a certain pair of Sydney girls—at
least, they used to be girls—lately arrived in Lon-
don, Lord Sheffield cleared out to Cairo. He didn’t
want to play cricket on the hearth.

The Australian Natives’ Association
“spreads ” itself over its forthcoming “National”
fete, and offers prizes up to fifteen guineas to be
competed for by amateur poets, novelists,
essayists, &e., who have to pay entrance-fees up
to ss. The A.N.A.’s patriotism is largely of the
pawnbroking order. Anyhow, sweeps of the
above character are distinctly illegal.

beats Sydney.

women’s tug-of-war.” Pratt, M.L.C. : “After
seeing it.” Pratt, philosopher and wit.
• * *
Chinamen have no nerves. Jimmy Tong,
hanged at Armidale, N.S.W., last week, died
with the impassiveness of his fatalistic race, to the
obvious regret of the local Pulseokd’s gallows-
reporter:—“Those who looked for haggard
features and rolling eyeballs and a loudly-protest-
ing tongue, looked all in vain.”

Meteorologist Wragge (Q.) hopes, when
his scheme for charting observations is complete,
to be able to predict the weather for six months
ahead. Tt is done in India with fair success.
# # #
Only a score of so of S.A. legislators
(including the Adelaide Club contingent, the
Speaker and the Ministers) accepted Governor
Kintore’s last invitation to dine at Government
House, Adelaide. The pachyderms of the S.A.
Assembly have, therefore, been actually skin-
pierced by Kintore’s former lofty hauteur.
Milord used to let his aide-de-camp and other
flunkeys' do the “receiving;” then, when dinner
was announced, he would stalk into the dining-
room, sit poker-back at the head of the table
during the melancholy courses, rise and propose
“ The Queen,” and stride majestically out again.
Vet, at this last feed, Dr. Oookburn, and other
alleged democrats actually turned up.
# # «
Albert Edward is not so cocksure as
Primate Smith, of Australia. When some of his
Cornish subjects asked H.R.H. for the appoint-
ment of a particular clergyman to a living within
his gift, lie did not say they were rebelling against
the decrees of the Lord, but sent them the man
they wanted.
Mrs. Sarah Makin explains that “there is a
God in Heaven, and she will get justice in the
Higher Courts ” :
Dangar’s anti-barmaid motion (adopted) in
N.S.W. Assembly was simply one to enable lion,
members to consider the barmaids how they gro»’.
Small wonder it was carried ! But when Tottie
is in any serious danger she will be championed
sturdily by a full House. The other evening
only half-pius-one voted, and that vote was to
bring up Tottie for further consideration.
* * *
When the Dixon (Melb.) will-case had
been eventually settled out of court one of the
counsel remarked, without a blush, that he and
his brother vultures were the only persons who
didn’t gain by the compromise. That is to say,
the estate amounted to about .£40,000, and only
20 per cent, had been absorbed in legal expenses
when the game was stopped. The Law expected
to gnaw away at the property till nothing was
left.
* * *
At Roma (Q.) a white labourer-looking
man unsuccessfully appealed to every local minister
and to the P.M. that he might be legally married
to a young aboriginal gin. Result: The couple
went off towards Charleville and—listen, dear
brethren—are now “Living in Sin.”
* # #
A society man up South, notorious for his
successes with the sex, has, for his trump-card—
not money, for that is scarce, nor jewellery, for he
has none to give away, nor his looks, which are
but passable—but a dog. He owns a magnificent
St. Bernard which he trots round with him on the
block. The women turn to admire the dog and
are undone in one act. The overgrown poodle has
a lot to answer for. Cave canem !
• * *
Almost any man would fly to drink on
reading at breakfast in the S. M. Herald :
No collision between Biela’s comet and the earth took
place yesterday.
The Herald would get up on its hind legs on the
morning after the sounding of the last trump
and report verbatim the tea-fight speeches made
on the Day of Judgment.
* # *
Justice A’Beckett practically discharged
the prisoners, Sarah Mills and William Reernan,
found guilty of perjury at Melbourne. The couple,
who were living together, swore in court that they
were man and wife, with no further object than
to hoodwink Mrs. Grundy, the Crown' prosecu-
trix. There is some talk of a second charge—
“ malappropriation of Respectability.”
# # #
Mrs. Dixson, relict of the queer testa-
ment-maker, “denied that her mother eloped to
Australia with a boundary-rider, or that she died
of drink.” Which excerpt from the press reports
is one small illustration of the brutal latitude the
courts permit to every legal bully.
» * #
The Aileshury baccarat case reminds a
correspondent of an affair which occurred some
years ago in Sydney when Mr. Alec. Finlay, then
in his heyday as a sportsman, lost over £2OOO to a
betting man named Clark, at tossing, in Emer-
son’s oyster-shop, and gave a cheque for the
amount. This did not suit Clark, who wanted
to know if the cheque was good. He didn’t want
no cheques off no one ; he had won his (Austra-
lian adjective) money, and he must have cash.
Joe and Barney Thompson and Bill Branch, who
were present, then offered Clark £lso'o for the
cheque, which he took. Finlay wanted to stop the
cheque, on the ground that he had been rooked,
but he eventually paid it, being advised to
do so by Mr. Herbert Power and others
who were present and saw the whole busi-
ness, which was, for a wonder, quits straight.
Clark played fair and wanted to leave off but
Finlay refused.
* # *
A Woolloomooloo ex-publican died re-
cently leaving some £20,000 to an adopted
daughter and charging the estate with £2 weekly
for the maintenance of the wife who had helped to
build up the fortune. The wife promptly refused
the “charge ” and faces the world again.

A MELBOURNE MYSTERY.
With bated breath the poet
To speak about a pair of legs,
The which a “ peeler,” prowling round,
Tied neatly in a parcel, found.
Now, where can be the ownah ?
The question of their age and sex
Enquiring minds appears to vex ;
Some said they graced a body male,
While others tell a touching tale
And vow a little donah,
Deprived of means to walk abroad,
Would like to have her shanks restored—
Wethinks we hear her weeping.
Who ran those limbs (Ah ! there's the rub)
That occupy a pickle-tub
In calm official keeping.
As certain as that eggs are eggs
The p’leeoe have copped two naked legs.
A person has been cut in halves ;
The outline of the famous calves
Suggests a dainty charmer,
And so some critics seem afraid
That probably a chorus-maid
Displayed them many times and oft,
To paralyse the chappies soft
Who love the lyric drama.
But why indulge in guesses vain ?
One awful fact alone is plain :
A man or girl ; alive or dead—
Matilda, Charley, Maud, or Fred—
Has had ’em amputated.
And since there’s not a creature who
Will come and claim the missing two,
The bard would modestly suggest
’Twere well to lay those legs at rest,
Or let them be cremated.
ENOUGH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are joys enough in this world of ours,
Though Fortune gives them not to all;
Crave ne’er to drink till the nectar sours,
Nor to feast on foods which a fool devours
Till the flavours on his palate pall.
When legs are strong, and pockets light,
A journey long o’er country rough
Has its wayside pleasures, and you’re all right—
Quite happy enough,
Not too happy, but happy enough.
When the autumn mists your ardour damp,
That onetime used to kindle soon,
Your legs may not be asked to tramp
So far and oft; perhaps in camp
You’ll sip your soup from silver spoon.
And the baldhead knows the mental ease
That comes to men who’ve made their “stuff”:
He mourns the loss of his youth, yet he’s
Quite happy enough—
Not too happy, but happy enough.
Poor Sarah Makin scarcely dreams
Of righteous treatment here,
For justice is not what it seems
In this polluted sphere.
She hasn’t faith in human love ;
The lady looks afar,
And puts her trust in One Above—
Where all the children are.
The babies warble hymns of praise
To Sarah’s solid worth ;
They sing about her tender ways
When they were yet on earth.
The Lord who rules that Higher Court
Has heard the evidence,
And read His servant’s soul—in short.
He knows her innocence.
If angels for weak mortals plead,
Preserving them from harm,
It must he very good, indeed.
To run a bahy-farm ;
For, as the cherubs quit the same,
They spread their tiny wings
And bless the thrifty farmer’s namo
Before the King of Kings.

UK, 171 & 557 GEOKGE STREET, SVJDNEV
a second-hand suit, dyed black, so that a cloud of
steam rises from him when he stands near a fire;
and the person he gets in trouble with is generally
the big and bullocky co-respondent, whose size
has led to the respondent’s undoing. In a recent
divorce-case the usual flabby little spy was hired
to obtain evidence for the plaintiff. He didn’t
get sufficient evidence, and the plaintiff used
unfriendly language while paying the bill. Then
the detective called round on the co-respondent
and offered, for a consideration, to give information
about how the perjury was got up on the other
side, and next instant there was a sound like
someone breaking open a melon with a bootjack,
and a partially-stunned private inquiry agent,
with a red stream flowing from his nose, was
sitting on the floor, and remarking faintly that a
man didn’t ought to be hit when he was down.
Another inquiry gentleman had an even worse
experience. He was employed by a publican’s
jealous wife to obtain evidence against
the old beer-seller sufficient to ensure a divorce,
and so he got himself up in what he thought was
an elaborate disguise and went out to live in the
hotel. Probably the disguise wasn’t so good as
he imagined, for the publican recognised him at
one try. He got no evidence, but he became the
bosom friend ot the landlord and spent over £3O
in standing drinks, and when even this failed to
evoke any information he attempted to introduce
some ladies of dubious repute to his new friend,
and then the beer-merchant knocked his head
hard against the floor .and put him out. The next
trouble was that the wife repudiated his bill, as
there were no results to show for the money
except a swollen head and a disordered liver; and
the final difficulty arose when the detective went
back to his assailant and offered to supply him,
for a small consideration, with evidence of adultery
against the wife, whereupon the enraged publican
took him across his knee, and, partially suiting the
action to the word, said he had a good mind to snap
his vertebrae for him. If this sort of thing spreads
there will be an end to the private-detective busi-
ness. The one fixed rule of ninety-nine|hundredths
of the trade is always to sell the plaintiff to the
respondent, and the respondent to the plaintiff,
and then to blackmail the co-respondent; but if
the parties won’t buy each other the sale must fall
through, and when they batter the detective as
well the last vestige of profit goes out of the
business.
* * #
The “change” trick, as run at Dubbo,
N.S.W. Decent-looking fellow buys from a
chemist sixpen’orth of eucalyptus oil, and planks
down half-a-sovereign. Is given 9s. 6d. change.
“’Ullo ! wot’s this ? Gave yer ’arf-a-suvrin, did
I ? Thought it was sixpence. Lemme look at it.”
The half-sovereign is handed to him, “ ’Ere, tell
yer wot, I might mistake this for a sixpence,
Ere’s sixpence more, sixpence and nineteen-and-
six is a pound ; gimme a note instead of this ere
change.” Chemist complies, and stranger exits
hurriedly. The arithmetical facts of the case
don’t strike the chemist for at least ten minutes.

A father advertises in a daily for his son,
who, in 1878, was “at Sydney, in the banking-
trade.” The old man is (naturally) “ anxious to
hear.” Nothing but the plaintive wail of the
amateur clarionet will suit this :
“Even the newspapers that support the
Gladstone Government condemn, in the most
sweeping manner, the unfairness shown by Mr.
Justice Matthew,”— (Argus cable.)—
The Maori language is a curious one,
depending largely on almost imperceptible inflec-
tions which the foreigner never altogether manages
to grasp. You may get up an elaborate compli-
ment in Maori setting forth that the recipient’s
eyes are like stars shining on the sea, and that you
want to fly with her to some verdant isle in a
region of eternal summer, and then you somehow
get just one accent wrong, and you tell that girl
with a smiling countenance that her uncle was
hanged for shoving a baby up a drain. A British
dude, who lately visited a native settlement, got
into trouble thus : He had formulated an expen-
sive and beautiful phrase by means of a dictionary
and a half-caste acquaintance, but it went wrong
somewhere or other, and when he bestowed it on
a native lady her friends cut off his retreat to the
steamer and chased him most of the way back to
the city by road.

The spiritual conductor of a Melbourne
faith-healing establishment is accused of kissing
his female disciples and curing himself with liver-
pills, instead of with silent prayer.

From Perth (W.A.) paper :
MARRIAGE.
TURVEY—MARTAIN.—On the Ist instant, at Cubbine,
by the Rev. H. C. George, Miss Hannah Martain, second
daughter of Mr. Martain, and second niece of Mr. C. H.
Martain, of the Royal Engineers, to Mr. Henry Turvey,
of York, and father of Manchester. The marriage was
celebrated at the house of the father of the bride, and a
very sociable and pleasant gathering was the result, and
one had it occurred previously would have caused our
present Ministry to have brought the railway through
Cubbine via York.
* * *
The Sydney Presbyterian: “Every
gambler is a thief. He is differentiated from
other thieves only by his method of stealing. But
all thieves are alike in this, that they desire to
transfer other people’s money into their own
pockets by one means or another.” Which
apothegm recalls to the minds of the unregenerate,
the poor parson and his collection-plate.
# * *
In Auckland (M.L.) you can now buy
false teeth on time-payment. You give the
dentist a mortgage on the teeth to secure pay-
ment, which is much the same as giving a bill-of-
sale over your liver, and then when the tooth-
smith tries to seize them for non-payment you
close your mouth and tell him through your nose
to take them at his own risk. He can’t get them
without an aggravated assault, and that means
damages.

In a N.Q. township
two youngsters were
haled before the magis-
trates (two big cattle-
raisers and a farmer) for
illegally pounding two
calves. The boys pleaded
guilty. The c a 111 e-
raisers were for the maxi-
mum penalty, £5O each
or six months’ hard.
The farmer asked for
leniency, urging their
youth, first offence, &0.,
but without avail. He
then asked the C.P.S. to
produce the record of
the last conviction for
a similar offence, saying
with emphasis : “It was
in March, 1888.” One
of thecattle-raiserslooked
amazed and then hur-
riedly said: “Ail right
—l’ll consent to a £5
fine,” and so it was de-
cided. Afterwards an
inquisitive vagabcnl
looked up that 1888 case
and found that the far-
mer was the prosecutor,
the cattle-raiser the cul-
prit—and the fine £5.
Such are the amenities of
the honorary magistrate
system !

A sign of the times is such announcements
as this, which are beginning to appear on the
brass-plates of the duly-qualified in fashionable
Melbourne streets: “Fee, with medicine, ss.
Families attended yearly, from £1 to £5. 9to 10;
12 to 2; 6to 7.30.”
* * *
South Australia has adopted the boarding-
out system with the State waifs and strays. It
works well as a whole. One noted baby-adopter,
a lady who has “ ’igh connections,” the other day
found out two of her “ boarders ” in telling a tara-
diddle, and punished them by tying mustard-
plasters on their mouths. The children were re-
moved from her “ care,” but otherwise the matter
was influentially hushed up.
« * •
The decisions in Brisbane Water Police
Court are grotesquely unfair. An officer, for
assaulting a seaman, is discharged with a re-
primand, or fined ss. When the case is reversed
the fine is £5, or six months’ hard.

Bendigo (Vic.) Advertiser has discovered
that a recent committer of felo de se was under no
“ mental disturbance,” but possessed “ a constitu-
tional tendency to suicide.” Thanks, awfully.
# * *
A church bazaar, going on in a Melbourne
suburb, is to pay for the parson’s pleasure trip to
England.
One of the horrors of the Civil Service is
the men who are always waiting with a gruesome
yearning for the dead men’s shoes of their
superiors. One of the big Government depart-
ments in Maoriland is a case in point. The per-
manent head of one branch is very old, and must
either die or retire ere long. The next man, the
one who should inherit the billet, has heart-
disease ; he may last 20 years or he may crumble
away at any moment. The third one on the list
has had sundry fits, and suffers from partial
paralysis, and there is a general impression that
the next seizure will prove fatal. These two
individuals spend most of their time watching the
boss and each other. It is a race between them—
or rather a race between the one’s diseased heart
and the other’s inevitable fit—for the old man’s
place. The rest of the staff is watching the three
of them, for when they all go off there will be
promotion all round. If a mad bull could be
induced to burst into the office, in all probability
the aged chief would flicker out right there from
nervous shock, and the second man’s diseased
heart would stop, and the third would have his
last fit on the floor at the same moment, and then
there would be jubilation among the staff. It is a
horrible thing when everybody in a big office is
waiting for somebody to die.
* * *
It was a considerate masher who accounted
for his not marrying one of four pretty sisters by
saying that he wouldn’t like to spoil the set.
* * *
A certain N.S. Wales M.P. (the son of a
still better known man), married his deceased
wife’s sister. The lady he was first engaged to,
finding that her sister was very fond of him, broke
off the engagement and went to England ; and he
eventually married the sister. She soon died,
however, and when her sister returned he married
her. The idea of a girl leaving her lover to give
her sister a chance is very pretty, but not original.
Dickens worked it up in his Christmas book,
“The Battle of Life.”
AN ILLUSTRATED CHESTNUT.
SAINT (to street-arab) : “You wicked boy—playing marbles on Saw-
bath. The Lord sees you.”
STREET-ARAB : “Can the Lord see me everywhere, Mister?”
SAINT: “Yes, you bad boy.”
STREET-ARAB : “Can He see me in our back yard, Mister?”
SAINT: “Yes, He can.”
STREET-ARAB : “It’s a lie, Mister, ’cos we aint got no back yarc. ’
O, tell ray sou an anxious tear
Along his father’s cheekbone flows,
I’ve lost him now this fourteen year—
Another tear rolls down my nose ;
Booffioo ! when last a sign he made,
He shone in Sydney’s banking-trade.
O, tell my che-ild his father weeps
To know how'e’er it fares with him,
And if his name unstained he keeps,
And is he sound in wind and limb ;
800-hoo ! I’m very much afraid,
The Devil runs the banking-trade.
O. lure my offspring to ray side—
I know you’ve got to find him first—
His youthful steps I fain would guide
From that broad road to Darlinghurst;
800-hoo ! I’ve heard colonials tell
The banking-trade is all a cell.
O, tell him, if he yet be free,
His father’s full of wild alarms—
I used to spread him on my knee,
I wait him now' with open arms;
800-hoo ! once more my son to clutch 1
Late Sydney news perturbs me much.
If he’d but joined a Melbourne Board
No tears his father’s nose had deck’d,
He might have grabb’d a glitt’ring hoard
And naught of consequences reck’d ;
800-hoo ! My cup flows o’er its brim,
I fear a Judge has wept o’er him !
OND DROMORE.
Even the papers don’t tell lies
To help the side their bosses favour!
This fact across the ocean flies,
Our morning cup of tea to flavour ;
A strange emotion stirs our breast,
We feel within new hopes arising—
And then we softly say “ I’m blest!
Well, dash it all! that is surprising.”
Most artless Argus not to know
That “ even ” is a full confession
Of journalistic falsehood, though
The blessed Truth is your profession.
How clean you give the game away
With one small word ! (and don’t intend it)
“ The thing’s so bad,” you really say,
“ The party's paper* can’t defend it."
SIR WALTER.
(Revised.)
O woman, in man’s hour of ease
And plenty, how you strive to please !
To win his heart—and purse—you try
With ogle, whisper, smile, and sigh.
But when he’s short of cash, you find,
You change your tactics and your mind ;
And from a fellow lacking “ oof ”
You deem it well to hold aloof—
Tip-tilt your nose and curve your lip,
And let the impecunious R.1.P.,
To find some other, wealthier, new man :
All this you do because —you’re Woman.
Warrego.
THE BREAKER.
The S.A. Government Gazette has had a
continuous existence as a weekly publication since
1838.
Up-to-date French story : A gentleman is
accosted by a footpad, who demands his purse or
his life. “Tenez, mon ami,” says the intended
victim, “ I demand arbitration.”
Ash, M.P., told the S.A. Assembly that
an “ objectionable ” Sydney journal, supposed to
be interdicted, was, for a slight extra payment,
sent to S.A. subscribers between the outside
wrappers of a Sydney religious newspaper.
At last Roma (Q.) Circuit Court, a China-
man was brought before Judge Paul, charged with
attempted suicide. His Honor, on discharging
prisoner : “Now, look here, my man, you made a
mistake that time. Next time don’t make the
same mistake —and fail.” But the Celestial
betrayed no sign of having seized the point.

FELTON, GRIM WADE, k Go’s
Patent Phosphorised Wheat.
As the season for vigorous action for the ex-
tirpation of the Rabbit Pest is now approaching,
the manufacturers desire to call the attention of
station-owners and managers to the latest ex-
pression of opinion they have received as to the
efficacy of their
PATENT PHOSPHORISED WHEAT
in the
DESTRUCTION of RABBITS.
“ To Messrs. Felton, Grimwade, and Co.,
Melbourne.
Dear Sirs, —
“ I am laying the Patent Phosphorised
Wheat now, and have better grass than
any in the district, still the rabbits will
take it in preference to the grass.
“ I cannot speak too highly of the wheat,
and have used it this last three seasons.
Last year I. did more with four bushels
than 1 did by the expenditure of ,£2OO in
carbon bisulph. and digging out. To
quote a neighbour’s opinion, it would be
cheap at a pound a bushel. I have given
samples to a number of persons in this
district, who will use it this coming
season.
“To conclude, there is only one way to
deal with the rabbit plague—by poison ;
all the exterminators and quack patents in
the colony, among which may be included
carbon bisulph., are not worth the tins or
oases they are forwarded in. The reason
is obvious—the time required and cost.
“The use of your wheat should be made
compulsory by Government, and the
rabbits would vanish.
“ Yours truly,
(Signed) “E. C. PETTCH,
“ Warra Warra,
“ Via Stawell.
“14/11/92.”
We Claim for Our
PATENT PHOSPHORISED WHEAT;
That it is Highly Poisonous.— By our pro-
cess of Manufacture it is impossible for one grain
to receive more phosphorous than another. Per-
fect uniformity of strength is thus attained!
Rabbits Eat it Greedily, as by this Patent
Process the wheat is made highly palatable.
Durability.— Each grain being encased in a
transparent and impervious envelope, it stands
exposure to the weather almost any length of time
without lessening its poisonous quality.
No Danger of Pii’e. —Careful experiments have
been made with wheat phosphorised to SIX
TIMES the strength of our wheat, and it could
not be made to fire.
