PAUL MELL.
Two mates were travelling through Queens-
land with pigs. Near a township, one said he’d
go in for an hour or so. Three or four hours
passed and there was no sign of his return.
Securing the pigs as best he could, the second man
started in to find his mate. At the first pub., in
answer to a question, the barmaid said : “ Oh,
yes, I know. You’re looking for the man with
the pigs.” “No, miss, I’m seeking the man who
ought to be with the pigs, but isn’t.” Which
over-smartness lost him, at least a finger of
whisky in his next nobbier.
Cairns Post takes a well-deserved “rise ”
out of an “affecting ” paragraph as follows which
lately appeared in a Sydney paper concerning that
well-advertised person, Pastor Gribble, the N.Q.
aboriginal missionary
Among the visitors of the Cairns Hospital came some of
the natives from the mission station, and a friend writes
of a very touching picture, one worthy of the shill of a
great artist: Mr. Gribble, sick in hod, propped up with
pillows, around the bed are gathered the blacks, the mis-
sionary is telling the old story of Christ’s love, and with
voice scarce above a whisper, broken by coughs, the oft-
told story is listened to with interest.
Broken by coughs! It makes us cough. The
latest Gribble advt. is contained in a wire to the
Sydney dadies that Gribble, jun., has only escaped
being eaten by three alligators through his prowess
as a swimmer.
Dear Bulletin, —Queensland has achieved
the honour of adding a new word, “swindlecate,”
to the English language. There are features
in the artistic jobs of our swindlecators, such,
for instance, ns the preference shares system,
which will well repay the study of artists in other
countries. The promoters of each land-grant
railway swindlecate usually receive one, or more,
preference shares, according to the service they
are able to render as legislators, or financiers, in
procuring the concession. Some consideration
must be paid for every share by law ; the price is,
therefore, fixed at =£l. A Queensland legislator
may not possess so much as one sov. in hard cash
at any one time, but his P.N. secured on his next
month’s salary would probably be accepted for
this sum. Extraordinary privileges are heaped on
these preference shares by the articles of associa-
tion. For example, in the Kimber agreement
for constructing the transcontinental railway, pro-
vision was made for forty preference shares, to
which was assigned one-fourth of the net profits.
George King, M.L.C., of Gowrie, stated in the
Legislative Council that the value of these shares
could not be assessed at less than .£60,000. The
inducement to the English capitalist is un-
doubtedly the acquisition of estates the size of
principalities which, exploited by slave-labour, will
yield princely revenues to himself and his heirs
through many generations. His aim is as old as
history itself - the large estates and slave-labour,
the latifundia and ergastulum of ancient Rome.
The inducement to the bankrupt and needy poli-
tician is the preference share, entitled to huge
profits extending over year's, which can be dis-
counted for a large sum in hard cash before
even a pick is put in the sod. Strange to
say, a careful study of the Act reveals thejfact that
swindles of even greater magnitude are pos-
sible. If these loopholes for fraud have been
left unintentionally, those most interested
will doubtless thank The. Bulletin for the follow-
ing wrinkle. The amount of land granted will be
in proportion to the cost of construction. The
land is valued at from one-fourth to one-eighth
its fair market-value. For instance, the projected
land-grant railway from Croydon to Granite
Creek via Herberton passes all the way through
perhaps the richest belt of mineral land in the
world. According to the Act this land may be —
and, of course, will be—valued at half-a-crown an
acre. Hence it follows that it is the interest of
the English company to which the concession is
sold to accept the highest and not the lowest
tender for constructing the railway. The higher
the tender the higher their ultimate profits. Into
whose maw will the stupendous profits made from
the railway contracts, let under this iniquitous
system, fall? There is absolutely no obstacle
to the following colossal job being success-
fully carried out. The small knot of hungry
politicians and bank managers who secure the
concession can float it in London for half
cash and half scrip. Their holding would
ba large enough to make a tool of the London
board. They could then re-form themselves into
a co npany of railway contractors and frame their
tender so as to yield themselves a colossal profit.
Their representatives on the Board would secure
its accept mce. The line before-mentioned is 350
miles long, and a moderate profit of .£lOOO per
mile spells ,£350, 00P in all. That some such
course was contemplated is evident to me from
the following facts. The Act expressly stipulates
that the company is not bound to adopt the
shortest route ((ho Government refused repeatedly
to survey the route beforehand) —the company is
not bound to disclose, the amount of the lowest
tender. The inference is obvious : the longer the
route the greater the prospective value of the
land-grab, the greater the present value of the
preference shares ; and last, but not least, the
greater the profits arising from the railway con-
tracts. Poor Queensland ! — Alario.

FUNNY IRISH BLUNDERS.
The native of the Gi’een Isle who said, “It’s
a great comfort to be left alone when your
sweetheart is with you,” and the Irish auctioneer
who, while praising some goods he was offering
for sale, exclaimed, “These are just the things I
should like to buy for my wife if she were a
widow,” made very witty bulls. Equally good,
but even more complicated, was that perpetrated
by the exile in America, who pathetically ex-
pressed himself thus ; “Sure an’ if I live till I die,
an’ God knows whether I will or not, I’ll visit
ould Oireland afore I lave Philadelfy.” A funnier
blunder still, was that made by a new-chum
Hibernian, who was a passenger by the Southern
Express the other night. He alighted, with other
thirsty souls, at Mittagong, but lingered at the
bar, over his favourite nectar, longer than he
should, and only regained the platform in time to
see the tail-lights of the train by which he intended
to have journeyed, receding from his view. For
a moment lie was dumb with astonishment, and
then collecting all the energy and presence of
mind he was capable of exerting, he cried, “Hould
on there, ye blatherin’ engine ; faix, there’s a man
on board oi ye that’s left behind!” These
stories have all the true Irish ring about
them, and call up memories of the Irishman
who complained to his physician that he
“gave him so many drugs he was sick a long
time after he got well.” No one who has used
St. Jacobs Oil can make a similar complaint to
that; it never causes a man to “feel sick after he
is well.” St. Jacobs Oil is an external remedy
for the absolute cure of pain, and it leaves no ill
effects behind. After using it the rheumatic
patient leaves his bed, or throws away his crutches
and begins life again a new man. Hear what Mr.
F. W. Kirby, of Mort-street, Balmain, says about
it: — “I use St. Jacobs Oil for rheumatism, and
do not know what I should do without it. The
kind of work I am engaged in compels me to be
out in all weathers, and I often have to remain in
wet clothes for hours together. The result is that
I get rheumatism badly in my back at times. I
find there is nothing equal to St. Jacobs Oil for
shifting it, and putting me all right again. Both
my wife and I look upon it as a real blessing.”
The voluntary testimony of thousands of such
witnesses proves that this marvellous remedy
possesses the confidence of all classes of the
community.

The Wild Cat Column.
The biggest Melbourne bankruptcy compositioii
is yet to come. A boomer now in foreign parts, who is
deeply obliging a lot of people by not returning, will have
to “ go” for about £3,000,000.
# # #
i'be Rutlisobild delegate to the Silver Confer-
ence warns the world that unless something be done to
give silver a fictitious value, the metal is hound to come
down to 2s. 9d. an oz., and possibly Is. 9J., and his only
remedy is to keep it up to 3s. 7d. at a cost of millions a
year to the participating nations. You might as we.l try
to give an artificial value to pigs’ feet. How he proposes
to meet the panic that would he bound to follow at the
end of the five years, the cable sayeth not. The result of
this conference will sound the death-knell of many an
Australian silver-mine.

They are saying now, in one of the Australias,
that when a money-lender with a Hebraic name is suing
an unfortunate client, it doesn’t look the square thing to
have nine Cohens on the Jewry, especially when the
balance consists of two Isaacs and a Solomons!).
The shares of the Amana Co., Lucknow, N.S.
Wales, were, it is cabled, over-applied for, and the floating
of other blocks is foreshadowed. It seems impossible for
the English people to let well alone. The Wentworth
property consists of some thousand acres, so that it the
British public are madly anxious for speculation there, we
can quite anticipate their anxiety being appeased. And
after that the deluge.

poly.

* * *
The everlasting old land-swindle. Cute politi-
cians and their hangers-on bought up land at £1 per acre
between Lismore (N.S.W.) and the Tweed, and the log-
rollers then got the Lismore-Tweed railway through the
Public Works Committee and Parliament. Now, those
land-grabbers ask huge prices for every acre which the
Government wants to “ resume.”

Some little time ere the crash, M. H. Davies
wrote a letter to the Melbourne papers denying a rumour
that he was in difficulties. He said he was .not then, had
not been, and was not likely to be so.
# »
That grand, old Maoriland mine, the Welcome,
was sold Hie other day, battery and all, for £7OO. The
Welcome, in its best days, paid its dividends as regularly
as sunrise, and for years ss. per share per month was by
no means an uncommon record. But it never built up a
solid reserve fund, and when the 4oz. stuff was finished it
started to flicker out.
* * •
Cov. Hopetoun says there are 12,000 empty
Melbourne houses.
* * *
It has just been decided in an English bank-
ruptcy appeal that a gift of diamonds to a wife, within
two years of insolvent y, constitutes a “ settlement,” and
as such is liable to challenge. It was laid down that
“any gift intended to he preserved by the donee” came
within the construction.
# # #
Alves’ wire-cloth ore-concentrator, cheap, effec-
tive, and simple, promises to fill a big breach in the
mining industry. The Alves’ effectiveness largely depends
on the use of a finely-meshed wire cloth placed on a
rubber bed. It is claimed that by their use mercury in
the stamper-boxes can be dispensed with as well as copper
plates, the free gold being saved in the lockers with
which Hie concentrator is provided, and that the rotary
action of the sand against the meshes has the effect of
concentrating all the minerals, and that these can be
separated by grading different tables. Splendid results
of working are shown. Whether so cheap and simple a
machine, requiring no motive power, will do all that is
said of Alves’, the writer hopes soon to have an oppor-
tunity of definitely ascertaining.
# * *
Not very long ago, when the expiring flickers of
the paralysed silver-boom went guttering out, that zigzag
journal, Sydney D.T., went baldheaded for a gold-boom,
prophesied it, hoped for it, advocated it, and crusaded it
as the one hope of Australia’s sudden salvation. Last
week it had an editorial anathema on “ The Gold Mania,”
in which it cursed the greed that followed the “ phantom
finger” of the gold fiend. Backslithersomeness, thy name
is King-street !
* # *
Regret is expressed by the Pambula corres-
pondent that the cyanide process was not used there
earlier. As we have said bffore, all that’s the matter
with Pambula is a want of sufficient goll, not of gold-
saving gear.
* « «
The rains, whicli have been abundant through-
out N.S.W. this year, have enabled the Golden Terrace
Co. to sluice almost continuously, although it is intended
to erect a dam at the head to conserve water in dry
weather. A# they get well into the gravel (hr yields
improve. They are now going upwards of 2s. to the
cubic yard. From the quantity of tailings already
accumulated near the river, we are afraid the “get
away ” will be their greatest trouble, and that ere long
they will have to await a fresh in the river to carry off
their rubbish.
# # *
W. B. Roberts, mining manager of Biggenden
gold and bismuth mine, Queensland, has written a letter
in which he gives some practical and valuable- informa-
tion concerning the relative cost of working h g and
small butteries i d ice which sbme N.S.W. so-called
mining men might well treasure up. Roberts’ own work
demonstrates beyond a doubt that he is thoroughly up to
his business.
* * #
A party of prospectors from N.S.W. have spent
some £2OO in trying to find an alluvial lead at Myer’s Flat,
near Eaglehavvli, Vic. If a lead of that kind had existed
there it surely would have been found long ago, when a
big rush took place at the Flat. The writer, then work-
ing in the vicinity, got there early on the morning of the
day following the striking of rich gold by the prospectors.
Wiiut might have been ! There were shanties, billiard-
saloons, and any number of stores erected, and at one
time 0000 men camped there. It was a rank duffer, and,
therefore, short-lived. Within a year there was nothing
of it left but thousands of shallow-holes.
# # *
The work of bringing water on to another
Shoalhaven sluicing property has commenced. The show
is at Bungonia, and consists of several hundreds of acres
with a tremendous fall into the river. The scheme is a
gravitation one and will cost some £20,000. Victorian
men, including Wallace, Syme and Jessop, are providing
the coin, and the management has been entrusted to Mr.
W. M‘Gregor, who is the principal vendor, and whose
faith in the field is firm. The property, like many another
near the river, was worked by diggers in former years in
a primitive way. In one part they used dry-blowers, and
there are numerous other signs that when the giant
nozzle is brought to play it will turn out big dividends.
# # #
The New Emerald Proprietary mine, it is
’■allied, has failed to float in London, and the representa-
tive who left Sydney laden with emeralds, the accumula-
tion of many mintin' work, is returning a raider man.
The Cremorne bore, Sydney harbour, has reached
1700 feet. The general opinion amongst coal men is that
the fault pierced by the first bore will be the fate of the
second. However, the borers are in a most noble specu-
lation. Success may mean millions.
• # *
Nearly all the big mines of Bendigo have one
visiting-day a week, when brokers, shareholders, and
speculators inspect the workings, and act on their own
judgment, but, as a rule, every important development
is known on the Exchange within an hour of its occurence.
Many mines are connected with the legal manager’s office
by telephone, so Hie result of every shot can be ascer-
tained.
* * #
A friend of the writer’s has received from
London some samples of mica with an intimation that
there is a good market there for the mineral if sent
according to sample. Unfortunately, however, the
samples are superior to any N.S.W. mica yet found.
Russia is the great mica-field of the world. Some good
shipments have recently been forwarded from W.A., and
we are told there are one or two mica deposits in S.A.
* # *
The English shareholders still hold on to that
awful frost, Mount Morgan Extended. It is now proposed
to increase the capital by £l2OO. What result that will
bring about, Heaven only knows for certain. True, the
“ mine ” is next (o Mount Morgan, and there are a number
of other wild cats, long since abandoned, which were
exactly in the same position.
# * #
Six months ago, the reports from Nambucca
Heads proclaimed that everything was right with the
mine—the ore was rich and abundant, but refractory.
Chlorination machinery alone was wanted to make it a
Mount Morgan. Now we are told everything is right with
the plant, and all that is wanted is richer ore. Anticipa-
tion, lOoz. per ton ; realisation, loz. Thousands expended
without the slightest prospect of a dividend.
# # #
Of the Killarney mine, a Pambula correspon-
dent says: “It assays 7oz. to the ton,” and adds—“lt it
only goes 2oz. in bulk.” That terrible “if !” Two years
ago, we remember ore from the mine assaying a great
many ounces to the ton, hut to get a ton of such-like ore
was the difficulty.
# « •
Two years ago Hobart was running mining
stock-exchanges day and night. Now, the last one has
been converted into a sporting club. Mining business
has absolutely collapsed.
• » •
The proposed Sunderland Coke and Coal Co,
has issued a very attractive prospectus.

The Broken Hill Consols mine is expecting to
pick up at a lower level the rich run of silver worked last
year, when £95,000 worth of silver was obtained from a
comparatively small number of tons. The shares went
up ; comparatively, they are now at zero
• • #
A new society, “The Provident Bounty Asso-
ciation,” has just been launched in London. It insures
against twins. Thus the Finance Chronicle :—“ What a
relief it will be to all those who are tormented with the
necessity of giving wedding presents to their young
friends, when instead of having to waste their money on
impossible inkstands and ornaments, they can hand in a
‘Provident Bounty policy against twins.’”—Mutual Pro-
vident Messenger.

estate will not exceed £130,000, and consist entirely of
unpaid calls.

Editor Bulletin, —This is the busiest season of
the year in N.S.W., yet trade is bad, money tight, labour
unemployed. What will it be in the winter, what is the
outlook, what the remedy ? An Act to permit mining on
private property would employ, inside a year, at least
50,000 men. What is the semi-paralysed Legislature
doing?—Australian, Harden, 21/11/’92.
# # #
Dear Bulletin, —I, a novice, want to keep the
report of a certain court-case out of the Sydney daily
papers. Can you tell me how it is to be done? That it
can be done is quite evident from what has occurred re
two oases heard in Sydney District Court on 25th ult.—
Sheerin v. Equitable Life Assurance Co., and Hennessy v.
the same. Defendants applied to the Judge to stop the
case being reported in the newspapers, but this applica-
tion was refused, yet although the case was opened and
then postponed to the following day, not a line ever
appeared in the dailies! I should have thought that the
fact of an application to stop newspapers reporting having
been refused by the Judge would only have made the
journals keener.—Sub.

The recent returns from the Rockdale freehold

The Peel River proprietary, Tamworth dis-
trict, after a long period of dullness, has at last had a
crushing of an oz to the ton, size of lode and extent to
which it has been opened unstated.
# * #
A Croydon miner writes of a place within ten
miles of a railway-station—an old alluvial field where
there is a hill entirely composed of alluvial wash contain-
ing gold from the grass-roots down to the bottom. His
lowest computation of the wash is Idwt. to the load, and
there are hundreds of thousands of loads available. He
adds that there is a river at the foot of the hill. He wants
to know if there are any moneyed men in Sydney prepared
for such a spec. It would be hard to find them just now.
A big revival in gold mining by reason of the inaugura-
tion of hydraulic sluicing has been here foreshadowed. It
will take time and money, hut it is coming as sure as to-
morrow’s sun, and our advice to the Croydon miner is to
wait and watch his opportunity.
* # *
It looks as if the Baker’s Creek Co. will this
time pot one or more men for gold-stealing, by reason of
one man turning Queen’s evidence. Gold-thieves at this
mine have long been suspected. As long as they confined
their attention to the ore in the mine all efforts to catch
lh‘iii have been futile; but to steal amalgam from the
battery, without detection, is not so easy, and (hinging
of miners’ clothes is advised, altho’, in a mine like Baker’s
Creek, the system would he practically useless because of
the number of the openings to the surface.
# # #
Another new explosive is to the fore —“ Storite”
—the tests (f which, on the Barrier, are described as
wonderful. We have heard of a hundred new things since
the introduction of dynamite which, for general use, still
remains king. Even one (including dynamite when first
introduced) had i.o “ noxious fumes,” and as the trials
were always made in the open air none were detected ;
but when the time came to try them in a hot cross-cut or
rise it was often found that awful brain • lank (what
hideous headaches and heart-pa’pitation are the result of
breathing dynamite-fumes 1) was their distinguishing
characteristic. More miners have been slowly done to
death by the fumes of explosives than killed by explo-
sions. The man who invents : n explouve more powerful
and cheaper than dynamite without noxious fumes will
deservedly become a millionaire.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
One of the Brisbane dailies won't publish
accounts of the local senior cricket matches. Reason:
“Undue prominence to sport.” But it publishes the
advts. of consultations and syndicates. These are paid
for, you see.
# * #
One M’Carthy thus describes football as played
in Ireland; —“ln Rugby you kick the ball; in Association
you kick the man if you cannot kick the ball ; and in
Gaelic you kick the ball if you cannot kick the man.”
* * #
At a recent S.A. race-meeting, John Lord, a
well-known sport, had three horses running. A party of
sportsmen went down to Morpbettville course by road,
and on the way passed an Advent Gospel-tent with a flag
bearing the inscription, “The Lord’s Day.” This was
considered “ a dead snip,” and John Lord’s nominations
were backed. All three won!
*" # #
The other day, at a Napier (Maoriland) race-
meeting, a Chinese rode his nag, “Seventy Bob,” in one
of the events. He backed his own mount, but “got left.”

* # #
It was Jack (“Doctor”) Hamilton, M.P. for
Cook, N.Q., who figured in the reports of the recent inUr-
colonial rifle-meeting in Sydney, N.S.W., as “Private
Hamilton,” and who won the N.S.W. revolver champion-
ship. In the “ Any Revolver Match,” Jack scored 41 out
of a possible 42. His six shots at 25yds. range were so
close together that a half-crown would have covered all
the marks. The shot that scored only 6 was just outside
the edge of the bull’s-eye. Hamilton is a fine specimen
of a well-preserved Victorian native. A score of years
ago, at the outbreak of the Palmer (N.Q.) gold-rush, he
was conspicuously the “best man” among some ten
thousand ; before that his feats on Gympie (S.Q.) were
phenomenal for single-handed pluck ; and it may be that,
even now, he would prove the best of any scratch ten
thousand men, take them as they come.

# # *
A new machine of the totalisator pattern has
been invented by Mr. T. T. Falvey, of Christchurch, M.L.
It has passed with credit the keenest scrutiny of the
Maoriland sportsmen, and has also satisfied all who have
inspected the working model at Melbourne. Should the
N.S.W. Totalisator Bill pass, which we trust it will, Mr.
Falvey’s invention will probably be given a trial at Rand-
wick and other racecourses.

Let cyclists take warning. George Melville
Kingston, champion cyclist of Dunedin, died last week,
in Melbourne, after falling over a dog while doing a
lightning training-burst in a public street. The writer
has more than once seen a lady knocked down by cyclists
racing in Melbourne streets, but he never yet saw a cyclist
pull up and make amends.

HER PORTRAIT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her hair, smooth and brown as the fur on the crook
Of the frond of a fern-tree, is soft as her eyes,
So liquidly bright with their innocent look
Of courteous enquiry or tranquil surprise.
She comes of high race, she is one of the daughters
Of a sire with a pedigree long as an earl ;
Her fine sloping shoulders, small head, and great quarters,
Stay—l’d better explain that “ she ” isn’t a girl.
No such luck: she’s a mare-could I only discover
A girl as kind, faithful, and bonny as she.
With sufficient good taste to take me for her lover,
I should not a lonely old bachelor he.
No bother to catch her, she looks on as idle
All dodgy endeavours from duty to sneak ;
She’ll put down her head while you slip on the bridle,
And rub all your shirt-buttons off with hef cheek.
She’s good on the ranges—no charger more steady—
Dowm a steep, greasy spur she will skid like a cat ;
For a jump or a scramble she always is ready.
But likes herself best on the beach or the flat.
She’ll sniff at the crossings and snort in the river,
When the bit is awash and the saddle afloat;
She’ll breast the strong current with ears all a-quiver,
And up the far bank she will spring like a goat.
Many a long day yet I hope to be carried
Scrambles and gallops on ridges or plain,
And should I some day have the luck to get married,
When my youngsters ride her’s I shall live then again
Gisborne, Oct. 23.
WAIHOA.
Testimonial Sweaters.
It is just about time that the testimonial-to-tho
esteemed-and-beloved-employer nuisance were
knocked on the head, and The Bulletin means to
do it, if constant hammering will accomplish the
fell deed. The thing is becoming a perfect scandal,
and will, if not checked, be worked into a highly-
valued branch of the art of sweating. It is quite ill
enough for the maintenance of manly independence
that any handful of men should bu in a position to
dole out employment—in other words, the right
to work—to their fellow-men. That fact alone
tends to the encouragement of servility. Mechani-
cal operatives have, so far, preserved their inde-
pendence of manner and of spirit less impaired
than any other class. But that is not because
they are a superior race, but merely because in
their occupations, to become competent in which
a special early training is requisite l , competition is
less severe than in some other occupations. The
common clerk—-the wretch who can only read and
write and cipher—is the most helpless and, conse-
quently, the most servile creature upon earth.
He is reared and trained in an atmosphere
of adulation and docility. He is taught
things by contraries, and every influence
presses upon him to shape his ideas to the
impression that all self-respecting men should
study to crawl with ease and grace. Only in
presence of inferiors is it proper to stand erect.
Not to bend the knee and the back before a
“ superior is an indication of ill-breeding.
# » *
When an employer of genteel slaves makes the
mistake of addressing, with the polite insolence of
manner which they are accustomed to, some
casually employed mechanic, it frequently happens
that the base mechanical person is instantly
stirred to surprised resentment, and tells the
tweed-snited tyrant to “go to hell,” or words to
similar effect. The clerk hears, trembles, wonders
and—pities the vulgarity and ignorance of the
follow. The tame man has been trained to such a
pitch of docility that he doesn't even envy the
freedom of the untamed man. Habit, surround-
ings, necessity, and example make of half our
young men moral geldings ; all the fire and man-
hood have gone out of them. They are content
to cringe that they may exist, and natural talent
is stifled and lost unless it be a talent fur syco-
phancy, in which case there is a chance, of promo-
tion, and, perhaps, emancipation and fortune.
* # #
It is almost invariably an individual so gifted
who sets in motion the scheme of a presentation
to an employer. Now, there is no sincerity in the
thing—as regards the employer. It is a dismal
effect of the existing system of society that
friendship or trust between employer and em-
ploye is almost entirely impossible. The better
able the employer may be to deal handsomely
with his employes, the less chance is thereof their
retaining any good will towards him. Every
salaried man in this world considers himself
underpaid. We defy anyone to find us a man
who does not regard himself as “entitled to an
increase.” Again, no man is totally without gall.
The obligation under which a servant is, of agree-
ing with his master’s sentiments, of testifying
towards him an admirable deference and regard,
of submitting to and even seeming to invite his
assumptions of all-round superiority, creates—
must create —reaction. The testimonial-promotor
does not least dislike the employer. It is not
affection which actuates him. It is self-interest.
The employer’s favour is necessary fur him : he is
cleverer than his fellows in matters of intrigue.
He will squeeze money from them to buy advance-
ment for himself, or, if not advancement, consoli-
dation of his position,
# # *
In respect of such matters the employer or
other influential person is nearly always imbecile.
He has found that his presence is greeted with
amiable respect; his conversation (when he has
deigned to address it to his servants) has been
received with gratification ; his views have been
endorsed with admiration for the intelligence
which framed them; his wishes have been
studied and often anticipated. He believes himself
beloved. The artful schemer plays on him as if he
were an organ. The trickster heads a subscription-
list handsomely—out of proportion to his salary,
indeed —and takes it round among his fellow-
slaves. The thing is inevitably complete as soon
as commenced. Who dare refuse to contribute ?
Who dare venture to be mean where economy may
involve him ? The promoter collects a handsome
sum, and devotes a proportion of it to getting a
long and abject lie engraved on plate or endorsed
in vellum, stating to the master how admirable,
how virtuous, how talented he is—not. This im-
partial and disinterested effusion from persons
whom he could plunge into penury next day, if ho
didn’t like the turn of an eyebrow, the expression
of an eye, the way the face was shaved, or the cut
of the coat worn, the employer modestly receives
and places prominently on his sideboard or hangs
on his wall. And the schemer who got up the
testimonial rests in mind as a deserving, trust-
worthy, and devoted soul, upon whom the broken-
meats of fortune will not be thrown away. The
miserables who contributed because they daren’t
do otherwise have converted the promoter’s
wisely-invested fiver into two hundred pounds’
worth of plate, and while they are too many and
too insignificant to bo borne in mind, baby has
gone without new boots, the wife has done with
last year’s bonnet again, every member of the
family has been deprived of something because
Mr. M ‘Sycophant had hit upon the idea that the
wedding of young Middlegilt furnished'a fine
occasion for advertising his devotion, at the
expense of the fools in the office.

affection of the Nerves, which affected me so much that

"OH DON’T TOUCH ME! DON’T COME
NEAR ME!”
These words were uttered with a howl—almost a
yell. Yet the boy to whom they were addressed
wasn’t within 10 feet of the howler, and wouldn’t
have come closer for his life. The scene was a big
business office in New York, and the howler was
the chief man in it. He owned the concern, and
was very rich, and a decent fellow enough. But
sometimes he would break out like that, and howl
as though he had just discovered a fire in a
powder-mill. You could hear him from the base-
ment to the roof. What was the matter with
him? Temporary insanity ? Not quite, but some-
thing nearly as bad. He had an acute attack of
gout in his toe, and at those solemn crises he
couldn’t bear the sight of even a shadow moving
in his direction. Ask somebody who has the gout
how it feels. Fancy a blacksmith twisting your
toe with hot pincers while a shoemaker is thrust-
ing a bradawl through your knee-joint. That’s a
■little like it.
Well, there are things not so bad as gout, yet
they make us touchy enough. Here comes a man,
for instance, who says, “Everything now was a
trouble to me.” What should he talk that way
for ? Why should everything have been a trouble
to him? There is an old saying that while we
can’t keep the crows from flying, we needn’t let
them make nests in our hair. That’s good sense.
But it’s easy to give advice and to quote proverbs.
How does a person act who suffers from boils ?
Now, the foundation of all feeling and pain is
the nerves. An hour or two of toothache is a
lesson on the nervous system. But there are
diseases (or one disease anyhow) in which all the
nerves in the body seem to tingle to every sight
and sound. The mind is on the look-out for evil
—the man is depressed and afraid. Every word
means mischief, and every bush hides an enemy.
So he thinks. He knows what Solomon meant
when he said, “ The grasshopper is a burden.”
Mr. Michael McCormack is a railway messenger
and lives in Mullingar, County Westmeath,
Ireland. In June, 1890, he was taken ill. His
mouth tasted foul and coppery, his stomach was
sour and dead, and when he forced down a little
food he felt so much distress and pain after it that
he was sorry he hadn’t let it alone and gone
hungry. Besides this, there were pains wander-
ing through his chest, back, and sides, hurting
him, biting here and there like ugly dogs loose in
a town. His head swam with dizziness, and he
couldn’t go to his work. All his ambition and
energy were gone out of him, and he would
scarcely have exerted himself even if he had been
suddenly promoted from the position of messenger
to that of station-master of the biggest station on
the railway.
“After a while,” he goes on to say, “a dull
heavy pain struck me in the back, so I couldn’t
stoop over. What I suffered from this and the
other things put together, I have no words to
describe. I had six months of it, and it was like
six years. In such a case a man takes medicines ;
all he is told about. This I did, without getting
any good from them, and I got weaker and weaker.
Everything was a trouble to me; I couldn’t bear
things I used to think nothing of.
“ In December, 1890, just before Christmas it
was, I first heard of Mother Seigel’s Syrup and
what it had done in cases like mine. I got a
bottle from Mr. Rogers’ Drug Stores, and before
I had used all of it I felt wonderfully better ; and
by keeping on with it a short time every pain and
ache went out of me, and I was able to go about
my work as well as ever I was in my life.” These
facts are vouched for by H. Rogers, Esq., Town
Commissioner, Mullingar.
Now, what made messenger McCormack’s
nerves so sensitive, and his life so miserable for
six months? Indigestion and dyspepsia; the
same detestable malady that does the same ill
turn for millions of others, men and women, of all
sorts and conditions. Plenty of them will read
this true and simple story, and our opinion is—
founded on the best of proofs—that if they try the
remedy which cured McCormack they will come
out of it as happily as he did. But the sooner the
better.
“Boxed.”
(FO R THE BU LLE T I N.)
Mo one who has not heard two experienced and
eloquent sheep-drovers airing their stock of plain
and variegated profanity over a “boxed ” mob of
sheep can hope to realise the feats performable by
the vocal organs of man. The earnest and ener-
getic sheep-drover, under ordinary conditions, in
the absence of excitement or vanity, can objur-
gate the eyes, hair, and tail of a dog and condemn
his soul and senses in a sentence that is like a
flash of chain lightning darting round a glass.
The uninitiated bystander hears and is thunder-
struck—filled with amazement and perhaps admir-
tion. It is only when two mobs under different
hands and bearing distinct brands became bo»ed
in consequence of bad judgment or ill-luck, and
are so hopelessly mixed up as to necessitate a
return to the yards and the waste of many hours
in drafting, that the drover really brings all his
eloquence into play and exhausts his resources of
fancy language in one vivid pyrotechnic eruption.
A boxed mob is the last thing in the way of a
calamity with which fate can afflict him.
Two mobs had just left the yards after the sale.
The rule of the road had not been scrupulously
observed, and number two was close upon the
heels of the first. Both drovers had cut the thing
very fine often before without precipitating a
catastrophe. Suddenly an ungainly wether struck
out of Bill’s mob, and led for Tom’s, followed by
a long line of sheep, impelled by that fatuous
confidence in leaders that reaches the sublimity of
idiocy in sheep.
A wild whirl of trotters, a sudden rush of dogs,
a cloud of dust, and over all the vivid flashes of
profanity from two strong men in their agony.
No good ! The dust passes off, and there remains
but one flock of sheep moving in a circle.
Now, Tom and Bill aim at sublimity of expres-
sion. The inspiration of great wrath is upon
them, and they advance towards each other,
belching red-hot profanity, and their gesticula-
tions are eloquent for bloodshed.
“ You ! ” or words to
that effect.
- Biff, bang ! They have met, and each reaches
for blood and hair. Their dogs are fighting
fiercely on the roadside, and the outer circle of
sheep go round and round and stare in dumb
wonder,
Tom and Bill fight blind, but with frantic
energy. Presently they go down plump. They
sit up in the dust, panting and full of bitterness,
and glare at each other, first with hellish hate,
then with curiosity, then surprise : —
“What, Bill?”
“Tom?”
They were brothers, and had met at last, after
five long years. Hands were outstretched as they
arose, but remembrance of the calamity that had
come upon them returned again, and they resumed
the fight with undiminished enthusiasm.
SILAS SNELL.
The Telegraph.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I am the child of the lightning and thunder,
Chained to the earth in the sight of my sires,
1 can but watch them when rending asunder
Mountains of cloud, from my prison of wires.
Still, in my comrades’ avenging defiance
I can exult when the tempests arise—
I am the s’ave of the tyrants of science,
Faster than Time in the light of their eyes.
Out from the towns and the dust of the cities,
There where the streams of humanity flow,
Out where the zephyrs are droning their ditties,
Over the ranges and rivers I go.
Careless alike of the burdens I carry.
Be they of pleasure, or be they of pain,
I have no leisure to weep nor to tarry—
Out through the sunshine and out through the rain.
Pattering and pattering and tirelessly clattering —
Sivifter than ever the swift winds blow ;
Clattering and clattering and tirelessly chattering.
Ever my burdens of weal and woe.
Out by the downs where the reapers are reaping,
Scenting the air with the perfumes of hay ;
Out by the towns where the sleepers are sleeping,
Over the mountains away and away.
Joy in my numbers and grief in my flashes,
Passions and feelings entrusted to me,
Fly from the heart, my dots and my dashes,
Over the mountains and under the sea.
Pattering and pattering and tirelessly clattering —
Swifter than ever the swift winds blow;
Clattering and clattering and tirelessly chattering,
Ever m y burdens of weal and woe.
Brisbane.
K.
OSTRACISED.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He may have been under society’s ban,
He may have been bad to the core,.
But he gave his life for a fellow man,
And what can a man do more !
PAUL MELL.
The genuine, hard-shell J. Bull in his
absolutely unadulterated state is a fearful and
wonderful thing to behold. A correspondent of
Adelaide Quiz tells how he met one of the species
at Renmark. He had taken up a farm right
alongside the main irrigation channel, and had
only to dig a small ditch or two to drown his land
in moisture any time he wanted it. But there
were no irrigation ditches in Hingland, and,
therefore, he didn’t w r antthe beastly thing. When
last seen he was patiently sprinkling his twenty
dry and dusty acres with water by the aid of a
pannikin and a colander, that being the nearest
approach to the solemn British watering-pot that
he could devise. An oyster walking upstairs
isn’t a patch on the genuine Bull, with the trade-
mark blown in the bottle as a guarantee against
imitations, when he gets under entirely new
conditions.
Fame?
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
After long months of growing,
So men of science say,
The ephemeron from its earth-shell
Is franchised, and breaks away.
Its crinkled wings a-drying
In summer’s gleaming ray,
For love it stays a moment,
Then ebbs its life away,
So after feeble strivings
Man’s heart cries glad, “ ’Tis fame !”
But his generation passes,
And passes his place of name.
F. J. HEATLY.

per week and expected to start work in summer at
4 a.m. Only a shilling a week more than Chaw-
bacon Smock in dear old Hingland.
t According to last census, one-sixth of the
L.S. population is foreign-born. But this sixth
contributes more inmates to the penitentiaries
than the whole of the native population.

A Loudon Hebrew money-lender a few
weeks ago failed to attach the wages of a school-
teacher. The debt, contracted in 1888, was £5,
with interest at £1 a month. The debtor had
paid £44, and owed £lB. Thrift!
So long ago as Jan. 3, 1851, a proclamation
was issued by the then Colonial Secretary Piesse,
of West Australia, offering a bonus of <£6o for the
introduction of one male and two female camels,
and £5O for the importation of two male and
eight female alpacas.
It is argued that N.S.W. Postmaster-
General Kidd’s Postal Amendment Bill is
weak in the clause enacting that every part of a
newspaper (including supplement) must be printed
from type set up, or stereos cast in the province.
That won’t shut out the matrices from which the
stereos may be cast.
Tennyson’s “ later poems ” have just been
published. One of them, called “The Church-
warden and the Curate,” is in dialect, a la the
“Northern Farmer.” An old country church-
warden, in his early days a Baptist, is advising
the new country curate, and after describing how
the Baptists came whilst he was ill and baptised
people in his pond, he says
8a I sticks like the ivin asking as I live to the owd church
now,
For they weshed theer sin i’ my pond, and I doubts they
poisoned the coo.
Dear Bulletin,— Re your paragraph anent
the old “Emily Downing,” let me add a few
afterwords. She continued one of the few whalers
faithful to the end. With her hold bricked up to
keep out the damp, she made her periodical
picnic trips provisioned with salt-horse, rotten
potatoes, and weevilly biscuit, as long as she
could decently hold together. When signs of
approaching dissolution became too apparent, she
was sold to the Tasmanian Government, and now
rides at anchor in Government House Bay,
Hobart, as a powder-hulk. Captain Willett (not
Welletts) was her skipper when she sailed as the
Lady Franklin —he brought the Norfolk islanders
up to Hobart Town, in her, in ’54. The captain’s
widow died at Newtown (Tas.) only last year.
Downing is still alive, and replete with wicked
old stories of the early times. His partner did
much better than he out of their joint whaling
business. — Van Demon.

Bonds.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
For me, all unaware,
At evening when the sun was nigh to set,
Love, the beguiler, spread a shining net,
Woven of a maiden’s hair.
“So flue the mesh, so light,
Ungentle ’twere to strive ” ; I let it be:
But from its web sweet hours twined stealthily
A threefold cord of might.
Since when, past force to break,
Love, Time, and Thou have forged the golden chain
I bear in these days, proudly, cho’ in pain ,
Gladly, for thy dear sake.
October 14, 1892.
A. E. WYNTON.
At latest advices there is a fearsome wash-
ing of dirty linen among the inmates of “ Beth-
shan,” Preacher Abbott’s faith-healing establish-
ment in Melbourne. Among other things it is
alleged that the brothers and sisters habitually
“kissed each other good night”—the kiss of
peace, not the sinful, buggy-muggy business of the
unregeuerate; that a sister mended the pastor’s
trousers and heaved them into his room at a late
hour ; that the pastor kissed a “dear girl ” on the
forehead and “looked upon her as a child of his
own ”; that a young lady got into the house some-
how one night, and was found sleeping on the sofa
at an early hour in the morning; that one
brother wanted to “ compel ” the sisters to go
to bed and “ submit to be anointed privately ; ”
that another brother cast reflections on the
Pastor’s morality ; that a spiritualist accused the
Pastor in a vision of having a small harem in
Brunswick ; that the Pastor didn’t kiss his wife
but kissed the other sisters instead, and that the
lady shook her fists at him. These matters having
been inquired into, the congregation passed a vote
of confidence in Pastor Abbott, and sang the
doxology, “ after which they hung about the
building in knots discussing the nauseous sub-
ject,” calmly says the Age —after itself devoting a
whole column of small type to the savoury morsel.
Prof. Allen builds an elaborate narrative
on the four severed limbs recently found bagged
in Melbourne. It reads like one of the detective
tales of Sherlock Holmes :
The man was of short stature, about fifty years of age, a
sufferer repeatedly from gout, unaccustomed to do heavy
work with his hands, did not wear rough boots, nor was
he a tramp. He was dead at the time the body was dis-
membered, but only for a few hours, and his death was
one involving a struggle, occurring in a park or field with
willow leaves about. The dismemberment of the 1 ;gs
had taken place but a little time before the parcel con-
taining them was found, and had been done in daylight,
the victim being laid on his back and then turned over to
complete the process. The man who had dismembered
the body was powerful, and used a heavy, sharp, narrow-
bladed knife; he had no professional skill in dissecting
the huimn body, and had considerable difficulty incon-
sequence ; though he went about the ghastly work with
deliberation. The exact period of death is clearly in-
dicated by rigor mortis not having set in—the legs having
but barely become cold—and by the blood continuing to
drip for some time after the legs had been found.
Likewise we believe a microscopic examination of
the hair on the arms showed that deceased was a
Primitive Methodist and had an uncle in Ger-
many, also it was deduced from the appearance of
his nails that he took little interest in politics and
contemplated taking up a selection in the mallee
district.

doctors.
Minister Lyne’s remark as to the secret
enemy of the N.S.W. Ministry who, while posing

at least a dozen members.
The latest National Ass move to dis-
credit the labour unions. An individual in
the pay of the pastoralists assumed the role of a
Labour man and organised the scum of the district
into a workers’ league. They nominated a labour-
candidate, and also an emergency man if the first
did not run. It has transpired that the favoured
candidate has served three years for bestiality,
and the second, six months “on account of a
watch.” This discovery has been made in time,
and the other side is hoist with its own petard.
In Loudon recently a dog ran up to a man
in tfie street, snatched his umbrella from him, and
bolted. Later in the day the dog’s owner was
arrested for drunkenness, and with him was found
a bundle of umbrellas. He had trained his tyke
to felony.

CONSUI/TATION PEER
The Boss of the "Admiral Lynch.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Did you ever hear tell of Chili ? I was leadin’ the
other day
Of President Balmaceda and of how he was sent
away.
It seems that he didn’t suit ’em—they thought
that they’d like a change,
So they started an insurrection and chased him
across the range.
They seem to be restless people—and, judging by
what you hear,
They raise up these revolutions ’bout two or three
times a year;
Arid the man that goes out of office, he goes for
the boundary quick,
For there isn’t ilo vote by ballot—it’s bullets that
does the trick.
And it ain’t like a real battle; where the prisoners’
lives are spared,
And they fight till there’s One side beaten and
then there’s a truce declared,
And the man that has gdt the licking goes down
like a blooming lord
To hand in his resignation and give up his bloom-
ing sword,
And the other man bows and takes-it, and evory-
_ thing’s all polite—
This wasn’t that kind of a picnic, this wasn’t that
sort of a fight.
For the pris’ners they took—they shot ’em ) no
odds were they small or great.
If they’d collared old Balmaceda, they reckoned
to shoot him straight.
A lot of bloodthirsty devils they were—-but there
ain’t a doubt
They must have been real plucked ’uns—the way
that they fought it out,
And the king of ’em all, I reckon, the man that
could stand a pinch,
Was the bos&of a one-horse gunboat. They called
her the “ Admiral Lynch.”
Well, he was for Balmaceda,, and after the war
was clone,
And Balmaceda Was beaten and his troops had
been forced to run,
The other man fetched his army and proceeded to
do things brown,
He marched ’em into the fortress and took com-
mand of the town.
Cannon and guns and horses troopin’ along the
road,
Rumblin’ over the bridges, and never a foeman
showed
Till they came in sight of the harbour, and the
very first thing they see
Was this mite of a one-horse gunboat a-lying
against the quay.
And there as they watched they noticed a flatter
of crimson rag,
And under their eyes he hoisted old Bahnaceda’s
flag.
Well, I tell you it fairly knocked ’em—it just took
away their breath,
For he must ha’ known if they caught him, ’twas
nothin’ but sudden death.
An’ he’d got no fire in his furnace, no chance to
put out to sea,
So he stood by his gun and waited with his vessel
against the quay.
Well, they sent him a civil message to say that
the war was done,
And most of his side were corpses, and all that
were left had run;
And blood had been spilt sufficient, so they gave
him a chance to decide
If he’d haul down his bit of bunting and come on
the winning side.
He listened and heard their message, and answered
them all polite,
That he was a Spanish hidalgo, and the men of
his race must fight I
A gunboat against an army, and with never a
chance to run,
And them with their hundred cannon and him
with a single gun,
The odds were a trifle heavy—but he wasn’t the
sort to flinch,
So he opened fire on the army, did the boss of the
“ Admiral Lynch.”
They pounded his boat to pieces, they silenced his
single gun,
Atid captured the whole consignment, for none of
’em cared to run ;
And it don’t say whether they shot him —it don’t
even give his name—
But whatever they did I’ll wager that be went to
his graveyard game.
I tell you those old Hidalgo's so stately and so
polite,
They turn out the real Maginnis when it comes to
an uphill fight.
There was General Alcantara, who died in the
heaviest brunt,
And General Alzereca was killed in the battle’s
front;
But the king of ’em all, I reckon —the man that
could stand a pinch—
Was uie man who attacked the army with the
gunboat “ Admiral Lynch.”
THE BANJO.
Bpar Bulletin, — I was visited the other
■day by a swagman with a visiting-card. He was
the only swagman with a card I ever heard of.
When I opened the door he placed the mouldy
doeumjfnt in my hand saying briefly, “Please
read bpth sides.” On one side was the name in
■copper-plate, “Henry A. Schmidt.” On the
■other I read as follows : “I, Henry A. Schmidt,
have no family to support. I am not a man of
■education in reduced circumstances. I am not
ipoking for work or yearning for a chance to earn
Ihy liquid and solid nutriment. I know I have not
been washed for a month. Don’t offer me .0 job.
I am simply on the burst, tear, ran-tan, razzle-
dazzle, bender, &c. For the sake of golden Truth
■give, me some money to spend in drink and I’ll
make a beast of myself.” That was all. I gave
him balf-a-crown and told him to take his carcase
from my door and his breath from off my
verandah, and then the only candid and truthful
swagman I ever saw shuffled away down the
stree f and made for the most adjacent pub.
Yours, c., Brick- a-Beack.

Postponed Sine Die.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The brothers quarrelled—over a woman, of course.
A straight question and curt reply led the differ-
ence up to its-intensity. The younger turned and
left with a passionate curse. The day on which
his brother saved him from drowning was for-
gotten, and a thousand kindnesses once remem-
bered with affection were now consigned to the
oblivion of “ perishing dreams.” He would leave
home!
A middle-aged man sat at moonrise smoking near
a dilapidated tent under an immense eucalypt.
The light grew stronger and more silvery and, sil-
houetted against its brilliance, the surroundings
struck him with a sense of lonely wretchedness.
Gaunt, gnarled trees, with few branches and
scanty foliage, rugged ridges ribbed with jagged
rocks showed Nature’s penury ; the only softening
touch —the stream where lie cradled by day. Yet
here he had found what humanity strives for
father than ambition’s laurels—gold. “ I will
give up ilext week, and go and find out Jack and
make it dp.”
The nloon \Vas beclouded, the sleeper’s ster-
torous breathirig deased avVhild as pleasant dreams
of earlier days coursed through his brain.
A sdowl darkened his fade as he enacted that
quarrel scene agairi, arid this Was chased by a
happy smile as the dream of reconciliation and
future content evoked the murmur, “Jack, let us
forgive!”
Jack came. The curtain • door was raised, a
figure crept in, a calm, benevolent face bent over
the smiling sleeper and earnestly scanned the
well-known features. As the dreamer muttered
inaudibly, the stooping one straightened and
passed out.
What a re-union there would be in the morning !
And what a surprise the now sleeping brbther
would receive !
Alas for human hopes ! The dreamer awoke to
a glorious day, and, rising, felt for his pipe.
Gone ! Watch, too —gold—everything of value.
Arid so was Jack. He had postponed the recon-
ciliation.
THE ORPHAN.
Writes “ Steele Grey” : “Deaf Bulletin,
—ln reply to Scotty W.’s letter in issue of 19th
Nov., concerning my remarks on Madeline Smith,
were I to take his wagers seriously, I might rea-
sonably request him to forward, as per offers, one
good saddle hack and one litter of fox-terrier
pups. The lady asserted by me to be identical
with Madeline Smith was so identified by four
different persons to my own knowledge—three of
whom had not, at the time of first seeing her,
previously known she was in Australia. One of
these saw her over and over again in Glasgow,
wherehe served hisapprenticeshipand practised his
trade. He and two of the others were in Glasgow
at the time of the trial. I did not myself see her
before she left Scotland, having, unfortunately, not
been born at the time. But let me review Scotty
W.’s allegations seriatim. (1) ‘ That Madeline
Smith never married a doctor, clever or other-
wise.’ Well, whether she married the doctor or
not, she lived with him as his wife for many
years. He was certainly reputed to be distinctly
clever, and held the following excellent diplomas
—M.R.C.S. Eng., L. et L. Mid. E.P.S. Glas.,
L.S.A. Bond. (2) ‘That she never drove any
doctor to drink.’ Whether she was or was not the
prime cause of his drinking habits, their continual
quarrelling, of which her temper was one of the
chief causes, aggravated the habit exceedingly.
(3) ‘That whether masterful or mistressful, she
was a jolly nice girl once.’ Browne and Stewart
(‘ Trials for Murder by Poisoning,’ p. 296) remark
‘she was attractive in person.’ She was cer-
tainly still a handsome woman 25 years after
the trial, i.e., at the age of 46. (4) ‘That
she has no property in Melbourne under
any other name than Smith.’ This is a
question of fact which would require too much
time to definitely settle. But I can tell Scotty an
illustrative little story about her property in the
seaside village I spoke of, which throws a curious
light on item 2. She there, under her married
name, owned a pretty little villa, where she and
the doctor lived. After their last big quarrel she
came up to Melbourne, sold the villa over her hus-
band’s head, and left for England. She sold to a
well-known sharebroker, from whom the doctor
bought it back, but, being unable to meet his pur-
chase bills when due, he forfeited. The villa is
now owned by a gentleman who is brother to the
village storekeeper and to a well-known Melbourne
clergyman. (5) ‘ That if she did poison the
Jersey-man, she got rid of a contemptible sneak,
who threatened to show her letters.’ On
his own showing, L’Angelier was a liar,
a coward, and a systematic seducer. (Browne
and Stewart, pp. 295-6). Also, he certainly
threatened Madeline that he would show her
father and her intended husband her letters. He
also told the Erench Consul at Glasgow that he
had them. The reason of her anxiety to get them
out of his hands or to get rid of him may be
gathered from these extracts : “To my copy is an
appendix of the whole of the letters, including
those suppressed in court, published in New York
at the Astor Press. Happdy it is not necessary to
dwell on their disgusting details,” Browne and
Stewart, p. 294. Also, in the charge of the Lord
Justice Clerk : ‘ passionate letters written by the
prisoner, declaring such strong love for L’Angelier,
and some of them expressed in very licentious
terms.’ (6.) ‘That the case being ‘not proven,’
the notoriety is also not proven.’ It is the very
fact of the ~ ‘ not proven ’ verdict that makes the
notoriety, for that form of deliverance is so rare.
It does not mean the same as ‘acquitted,’
by any means, which fact is well illustrated
in this very case, for on the first count the verdict
was ‘ not guilty,’ whereas on the second and third
it was ‘not proven.’ The notoriety is further
shown by the fact that it is the arsenic case in
British law, and quoted in .11 the leading text-
books, e.g. Taylor (ed. Stevenson), and Browne,
and Stewart, the latter giving nearly 70 pages of
it. (7) ‘That she was not seen in Melbourne a
week or a month ago, or since about 1870.’ At
the time I wrote, someone had mentioned casually
to me that he thought he had seen Mrs. Hr.
a day or two back. I, myself, saw her many
times between 1879 and 1882 (so did the four
others above mentioned) both in Melbourne and
the sea-side village. She was at the latter place
as late as 1884. Furthermore, Madeline Smith’s
‘not proven’ verdict was due more to an extremely
clever girl and her extremely clever advocate than
to innocence. She was proved to have motive,
means and opportunity for compassing the Jersey
man’s death, and the weak point in the chain was
the absence of complete proof of their meeting on
that fatal 22nd of March.”—Yours truly, S. G.

Easily Killed
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OLLY’S pipe had gone out
between his fingers, and he
stared lugubriously into the
glowing embers, unusually
silent and meditative. We
had not lit the candle, and
the hut was illuminated only
by the flames of the fire. I
smoked and dreamed about
the golden reef we were going
to find “ when luck turned.”
Bill was the first to break the silence :
“ Didyeh ever killaman, Nig ? ”
Golly had the bad Australian habit of joining
his words together, and his conversation ran in
strings of various lengths.
“No, but if you sit there for
another ten minutes, looking as
miserable as an owl, I might accom-
modate you.”
“ Don’ mind met’night, ole chap.
This night six years ago I killed a
man, and I alwus feel a bit off on
the 14th of June.”
“ It’s the least you might do;
but are you joking ?”
“No. It’s the easiest thing in
the world to kill a (man. I saw a
chap slip off a tip’at Dunolly there,
and fall about 18 inches, and they
picked him up dead.”
“Not so easy. Tom Belton fell
down a prospecting shaft over near
Whipstick, and came up in the
bucket five minutes later, smoking
the pipe he held between his teeth
when he fell. When he went home,
he warmed up his wife because there
was something wrong with his vic-
tuals.”
“ That’s a fact, but my man died
easy—easy as puffing out a candle.
You was surprised I didn’t fight
when Maloney threw the mug, over
at Kate’s, the other night. If you’d
killed a man with a turn of your
hand, you’d stand a lot before
hitting anybody.
“I was working on a Tasma-
nian field at the time. I was
about 19, and had a job on top, at
the battery. My hut-mate was a Maorilander,
named Fay, a decent chap, and a good miner
when sober, but he used to have bursts now and
then, when he took a lot of looking after, and a
lot of bush doctoring before he was well again.
He’d start drinking, you know, and gradually
drift into mild jim-jams, then rally off with a
wild tangle, when a fellow had to lose a shift or
two, and stay around, sitting on his chest most of
the time.
“ While Fay was sober he scarcely had a word
to say, and never whispered a hint about his own
affairs, but when his spree was a week old or so,
he’d start yabbering to himself about Nelly, and a
baby, and I reckoned he’d had a split with his
wife, and they’d separated on account of some
fault of his. My mate never wrote letters him-
self, but he was always looking for one, and,
coming off night shift, his first question was,
‘ Any letter, Bill ?’ but he got none to my know-
ledge, till that night when my cursed folly finished
him.”
Golly had picked up a stick, and sat with his
face averted, fidgetting amongst the embers.
“ I got to looking for this letter he was always
expecting as eager as himself, and when it did
come, and I saw it was addressed in a woman s
hand, I was as glad as a kid about it. I liked the
man, damn it, and hoped things would come out
right and comfortable with him. Fay had been
drinking two or three days when the letter arrived,
and I guessed it wouldn’t be much good to him
for some time. He came into the hut late that
night. It had been raining, and he was wet and
dirty. I had a good fire on, and stood with my
back to it, watching him. He staggered on to his
bunk, and sat there, with his head working, and
his fingers twitching and plucking at his coat.
There was a bark shelf like ours over the fire, and
the letter was reared against the tea-box, just
beside my head.
“ When Fay looked up there was a nasty glare
in his eyes, and he stared at me in a way that
made me shiver a bit. I was always nervous with
a man far gone in drink, and I’d seen Fay in
the humour for ugly things. Besides, I was a
youngster, and knew that my mate was near the
stage when he wouldn’t know me from a gohanna
or a bunyip. He got up after a bit, and staggered
over towards me, clawing like. If I hadn’t been
a fool I’d a just taken hold of him, and forced
him on to his bunk again. I was strong enough
to do it, but I was a fool, and when he came too
close, and seemed to be trying to grip me, I hit
out—not hard, only half a push. He went down,
over backwards across a candle-box, with ins head
under his arm, so he never got up again.
“ Dead as a door-nail! He’d dislocated his neck
or something. Good God ! what a time I spent
over him. He had the letter gripped in his dead
hand. It was that he was after, not me.
“The coroner came down from Launceston, and
there was an inquest. I said nothing about
having struck at Fay. I funked.on it, and told
them he had fallen over the box in drink; but I
gave myself away when they opened the letter,
and read it. It was from his wife. She was
living in Christchurch, with her father, and wrote
to say that she forgave everything, and wanted
him to come home at once, and they would be
happy again. She said she had not let their boy
forget his father.
“That finished me. I planked down in the
room, and howled ; said I’d killed him, that I was
a murderer, and wanted them to hang me, and
carried on like a lunatic. This made the case bad
against me, but there was a postponement, and
when the inquest came on again my story was be-
lieved, and a verdict of accidental death returned.
It’s easy to kill a man, Nig, but hard to forget
it.”
Golly dropped the stick, and hid his face in his
hands, and I went out and left him a while.
E.D.

Sydney Morning . Herald, “brushing
with hasty steps the Jews away ” from the path of
journalism, is a spectacle for gods and men.
Whatever may be said against them on other
grounds, no sane person will deny that Jews as
journalists are at the head of their profession. It
is your ranting Dissenter and your wily Christian
minister that play the devil with journalism in
Australia.
The story of a dutiful son is told in a
recent Japanese paper. His aged mother’s eye-
sight was failing, and the local witch or rain-
maker or debbil-debbil-woman told him that a
human liver was the only thing that could restore
her. Thereupon he decided to commit hari-kari,
and instructed his wife to hand over his liver to
her mother-in-law as soon as he was deceased.
The wife objected strongly and insisted on com-
mitting suicide herself. Finally they tossed for
it, and the wife losing, her husband reluctantly
choked her, and proceeded to cut het up, but the
police arrived in the midst of the operation and
ran him in; consequently the wife is dead, and
the husband is in gaol, and the old blind mothef
didn’t get the required liver after all.

A Poor Joke.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
you can’t count me in, boys; I’m off it—
I’m jack 0’ them practical jokes,
They ain’t neither pleasure ner profit,
An’ th’ fellers that plays ’em is mokes.
I’ve got sense, but I once was a duffer,
An’ I fooled up my share, I allow,
But since conscience hes made me t’ suffer—
She’s peggin away et me now.
“ You notice I’ve aged rather early,
An’ th’ wrinkles is deep on my face ?
That’s sorrer—l’m sixty-nine, barely,
Jes’ camp, an’ I’ll tell yeh my case-.
’Twas here on The Springs hi the Sixties,
We Was Workin’ tire lead on this spot;
An’ we was, t admit it betwixt yes,
A father unprincipled lot.
u We Was drunk most th’ day on th’ Sundays—
Thet licker is hell, I insists—
An’ our exercise mostly on Mondays
War feats of endoorance with fists.
See, the wash wasn’t what we called wealthy—
Ten pennyweight stuff, there about—
An’ we took matters easy an’ healthy,
Tho’ we’d rush for the same now, no doubt;
“ Well, one rnoruin’, frum over th’ border.,
Two Mongols moved inter th’ camp,
Which we voted a tiling out of order—
The climate for <3hows was too damp).
But it happened A couple of troopers
Arrived on The Springs that same week;
So the Chinks, in their opium stoopers,
Didn’t wander down inter th’ creek,
“ Or get drowned in th’ dam at The Crescent,
As we reckoned might happen somehow ;
But they settled down, easy an’ pleasant,
An’ there wasn’t th’ smell of a row.
Howsomdever, we wasn’t long twiggm’
Th’ Chows was a ignerent pair,
An’ knew nothin’ at all about diggin’,
An’ that was our chance t’ get square.
“It was ’cordin’ t’ Bastow’s directions,
Tho’ I volunteered fer th’ game,
Fer t’ collar th’ heathens’ affections,
An’ lay them right on to a claim
Round th’ bend where we’d bottomed a duffer—
Meself an’ Pat Foley—right there,
Where th’ sinkin’ is deeper an’ tougher
Than th’ holes of OehCnnar, I swear.
“That shaft was a reg’lar clinker—
I was cursin’ it on’y t’day—
Quite a fortni’t it took us t’ sink her,
An’ then we come through on th’ clay—
Not the ghost of a han’full of gravel.
Well, we dropped it without any fuss,
On th hill pegged the best we could snavcl,
An’ th’ devil could prospect, fer us.
“ Course tho Pagans was not a bit wiser,
An’ I counted it pretty fair game
T’ put up es their friend an’ adviser,
An’ induce ’em t’ take up that claim,
By a-crackin’ th’ lay an’ position,
So’s t’ get ’em t’ sink on th’ clay.
Till they struck a hot shop in Perdition
Or tapped water in Europe some day.
“ But th’ monkeys was mighty suspicious,
Wouldn’t have it I cared for their sakes—
Here I think thet all Chinkies is vicious
An’ hate ’em like fever an’ snakes.
Then I tried a new system of dealing
An’ offered advice et a fee,
An’ they caught on immedjit. Fine feelin’
Is wasted on any Chinee.
“ So they pegged out our cast-off, th’ duffer,
Their ‘rights ’ they had took out exact,
An’ Ah Kit, who was boss, wouldn’t suffer
Not th’ smallest neglect of the Act,
An' I put in their pegs to a fraction,
Es grave es a brick on a hob,
Rigged up things t’ their full satisfaction,
An’ charged ’em five quid for the job.
“ Well, th’ heathens soon set their pegs goin*,
An’ they seemed rather fond o’ th’ graft,
Tho’ th’ boys hed took trouble in stowin’
A heap o’ dead things in th’ shaft,
An’ we chuckled an’ thought we had got ’em.
I knew I could tickle th’ pair
T’ keep sinkin’ on inter th’ bottom
For gravel that never was there.
“Next night a most harrowin’ rumour
Went round, an’ th’ camp was half daft :
It wus said thet a nugget—a boomer—
Hed bin took by th’ Chows from our shaft.
Point of fact thet th’ Pagans had struck it,
Hed knocked down a sample o’ wash
Thet looked good fer a pound t’ th’ bucket,
An’ our joke hed gone hopelessly squash.
“ It was c’rect, boys, by all that is holy !
We’d hit a “ false bottom,”* sure’s fate,
An’ th’ fortune of self an’ of
Was scooped by Ah Kit an’ his mate.
We resolved thet these Chinese was sappin’
Th’ wealth o’ th’ land, an’ agreed
Somethin’ serious was goin’t’ happen,
When th’ troopers rode on t’ th’ lead.
“ Yes, we scrambled fer claims all around ’em,
An’ we made th’ foam fly for a week,
But th’ Chows hed th’ gilt edge. Confound ’em,
They’d lobbed right on top o’ th’ streak !
No, your joke, boys, I reckon is risky,
An’ somewhat ridic’lus, I doubt,
But I’m with you fer frien’dship an’ whisky
If one of ye’s game nuf t’ shout.”
EDWARD DYSON.
* It has happened in sinking on alluvial fields that a
streak of the strata which usually underlies the wash (the
“bottom”) has been found immediately above it, the
result of a geological freak. This has occasionally
deceived even diggers of some experience, and led them
to abandon claims as duffers which, when subsequently
sunk a little further, have proved to be golden holes.
There are moments in the lives of each of us
when, in the midst of unexpected, unforeseen danger, we
shall require (o exercise to its utmost extent all the
nervoui power we may possess. And there is no greater
foe or danger to happiness than debility, which ought to
be carefully warded off. In the following case Mr. E.
Robinson, of Tumut, N.S.W., gives his experience of it:
“ For the last three or four years I have been conscious
that my strength, and in an equal degree my spirits, were
waning. I was losing all interest in anything and in
everything. I could not account for it. lat last began
to suspect I might have the seeds of consumption sown in
my system, and that they were bringing forth fruit. I
saw your advertisement in the Sydney Herald, and
resolved to try what Marshall’s Tonic Nervine could do
for me—this was five months ago. I have completed a
course of 18 bottles, and I can truly say a man in better
health and spirits cannot exist. I eat well, sleep well,
and take any amount of physical exercise, long walks,
and feel no fatigue, and my strength is apparent to me in
every movement I make, every step I take. All this I
owe to you and your sovereign remedy. Let the world
know. You are only too welcome to publish this, in the
hope other poor sufferers may make use of your strength
giving fluid.” Will “other poor sufferers” hesitate now?
Marshall's Tonic Nervine is within the reach of all.

THE ESTATE OF
FONTAINEBLEAU,
WENTWORTH FALLS.
'THIS MAGNIFICENT ESTATE of 239 ACRES has been
■** surveyed by Messrs. Miller and Lewis, of Pitt Street,
Sydney, into 138 Allotments, ranging from 1 Acre
Residential Blocks to 10£ Acres, situate only 500 yards
from the WENTWORTH FALLS RAILWAY STATION,
on the opposite side of the line. It embraces all that fine
and elevated land opposite the mansion of R. M. Pitt,
Esq., whose splendid garden excites the admiration of
every passenger on the Great Western railway.
When the delightful climate of Wentworth Falls is taken
into consideration, the place may be safely described as
Tlic Madeira of the Blue Mountains.
Delicate constitutions and worn-out business men are
benefited equally by the refreshing purity of the (mountain
air, and the estate is not surpassed for residential purposes.
The FONTAINEBLEAU ESTATE
Is well described by the poet, viz.:—
The vvaratahj all gleaming red,
Lifting in pride its stately head,
, Shines through the clustering ferns ;
While as the wattle’s yellow bloom
trails in soft showers, its. deep perfume
The air to sweetness turns.
Would you this haunt of beauty know ?
’Tis the charming Estate of Fontainebleau !
The Blaxland Road is now being made from the Railway
fence, and, as it runs right through this magnificent estate,
it will, when finished, be without exception the finest
Carriage Drive on the Mountains.
The FONTAINEBLEAU ESTATE is the mdst elevated
and beautiful about Wentworth Falls —far and away
superior to the land on the other side of the line. The
picturesque views and the extreme fertility of the soil
renders the place very attractive, and specially adapted
for Fruit or Flower Gardens, Orchards or Dairy Farms,
for which Natdre seems to have intended it.
